<div class="mw-body-content mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr" id="mw-content-text" lang="en"><div class="mw-parser-output"><div class="shortdescription nomobile noexcerpt noprint searchaux" style="display:none">Language family native to western and southern Eurasia</div>
<p class="mw-empty-elt">
</p>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1033289096">.mw-parser-output .hatnote{font-style:italic}.mw-parser-output div.hatnote{padding-left:1.6em;margin-bottom:0.5em}.mw-parser-output .hatnote i{font-style:normal}.mw-parser-output .hatnote+link+.hatnote{margin-top:-0.5em}</style><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">"Indo-European" redirects here. For Eurasian people living in or connected with Indonesia, see <a href="/wiki/Indo_people" title="Indo people">Indo people</a>. For other uses, see <a class="mw-disambig" href="/wiki/Indo-European_(disambiguation)" title="Indo-European (disambiguation)">Indo-European (disambiguation)</a>.</div>
<p class="mw-empty-elt">
</p>

</tr><tr><td class="sidebar-navbar"><style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1063604349">.mw-parser-output .navbar{display:inline;font-size:88%;font-weight:normal}.mw-parser-output .navbar-collapse{float:left;text-align:left}.mw-parser-output .navbar-boxtext{word-spacing:0}.mw-parser-output .navbar ul{display:inline-block;white-space:nowrap;line-height:inherit}.mw-parser-output .navbar-brackets::before{margin-right:-0.125em;content:"[ "}.mw-parser-output .navbar-brackets::after{margin-left:-0.125em;content:" ]"}.mw-parser-output .navbar li{word-spacing:-0.125em}.mw-parser-output .navbar a>span,.mw-parser-output .navbar a>abbr{text-decoration:inherit}.mw-parser-output .navbar-mini abbr{font-variant:small-caps;border-bottom:none;text-decoration:none;cursor:inherit}.mw-parser-output .navbar-ct-full{font-size:114%;margin:0 7em}.mw-parser-output .navbar-ct-mini{font-size:114%;margin:0 4em}</style><div class="navbar plainlinks hlist navbar-mini"><ul><li class="nv-view"><a href="/wiki/Template:Indo-European_topics" title="Template:Indo-European topics"><abbr title="View this template">v</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-talk"><a href="/wiki/Template_talk:Indo-European_topics" title="Template talk:Indo-European topics"><abbr title="Discuss this template">t</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-edit"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:Indo-European_topics&amp;action=edit"><abbr title="Edit this template">e</abbr></a></li></ul></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>The <b>Indo-European languages</b> are a <a href="/wiki/Language_family" title="Language family">language family</a> native to the <a href="/wiki/Languages_of_Europe" title="Languages of Europe">overwhelming majority of Europe</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Iranian_plateau" title="Iranian plateau">Iranian plateau</a>, and the northern <a href="/wiki/Indian_subcontinent" title="Indian subcontinent">Indian subcontinent</a>. Some European languages of this family, <a href="/wiki/English_language" title="English language">English</a>, <a href="/wiki/French_language" title="French language">French</a>, <a href="/wiki/Portuguese_language" title="Portuguese language">Portuguese</a>, <a href="/wiki/Russian_language" title="Russian language">Russian</a>, <a href="/wiki/Dutch_language" title="Dutch language">Dutch</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Spanish_language" title="Spanish language">Spanish</a>, have expanded through <a href="/wiki/Colonialism" title="Colonialism">colonialism</a> in the modern period and are now spoken across several continents. The Indo-European family is divided into several branches or sub-families, of which there are eight groups with languages still alive today: <a href="/wiki/Albanian_language" title="Albanian language">Albanian</a>, <a href="/wiki/Armenian_language" title="Armenian language">Armenian</a>, <a href="/wiki/Balto-Slavic_languages" title="Balto-Slavic languages">Balto-Slavic</a>, <a href="/wiki/Celtic_languages" title="Celtic languages">Celtic</a>, <a href="/wiki/Germanic_languages" title="Germanic languages">Germanic</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hellenic_languages" title="Hellenic languages">Hellenic</a>, <a href="/wiki/Indo-Iranian_languages" title="Indo-Iranian languages">Indo-Iranian</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Italic_languages" title="Italic languages">Italic</a>; and another nine subdivisions that are now <a href="/wiki/Extinct_language" title="Extinct language">extinct</a>. 
</p><p>Today, the individual Indo-European languages with the most native speakers are English, <a href="/wiki/Hindustani_language" title="Hindustani language">Hindi–Urdu</a>, Spanish, <a href="/wiki/Bengali_language" title="Bengali language">Bengali</a>, French, Russian, Portuguese, <a href="/wiki/German_language" title="German language">German</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Punjabi_language" title="Punjabi language">Punjabi</a>, each with over 100 million native speakers; many others are small and in danger of extinction.
</p><p>In total, 46% of the world's population (3.2 billion people) speaks an Indo-European language as a <a href="/wiki/First_language" title="First language">first language</a> — by far the highest of any language family. There are about 445 living Indo-European languages, according to an estimate by <i><a href="/wiki/Ethnologue" title="Ethnologue">Ethnologue</a></i>, with over two-thirds (313) of them belonging to the Indo-Iranian branch.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1"><a href="#cite_note-1">[1]</a></sup>
</p><p>All Indo-European languages are descended from a single prehistoric language, <a href="/wiki/Linguistic_reconstruction" title="Linguistic reconstruction">linguistically reconstructed</a> as <a href="/wiki/Proto-Indo-European_language" title="Proto-Indo-European language">Proto-Indo-European</a>, spoken sometime in the <a href="/wiki/Neolithic" title="Neolithic">Neolithic</a> to <a href="/wiki/Bronze_Age" title="Bronze Age">Early Bronze Age</a>. The geographical location where it was spoken, the <a href="/wiki/Proto-Indo-European_homeland" title="Proto-Indo-European homeland">Proto-Indo-European homeland</a>, has been the object of many competing hypotheses; the academic consensus supports the <a href="/wiki/Kurgan_hypothesis" title="Kurgan hypothesis">Kurgan hypothesis</a>, which posits the homeland to be the <a href="/wiki/Pontic%E2%80%93Caspian_steppe" title="Pontic–Caspian steppe">Pontic–Caspian steppe</a> in what is now <a href="/wiki/Ukraine" title="Ukraine">Ukraine</a> and <a href="/wiki/Southern_Russia" title="Southern Russia">southern Russia</a>, associated with the <a href="/wiki/Yamnaya_culture" title="Yamnaya culture">Yamnaya culture</a> and other related archaeological cultures during the 4th millennium BC to early 3rd millennium BC. By the time the first written records appeared, Indo-European had already evolved into numerous languages spoken across much of <a href="/wiki/Europe" title="Europe">Europe</a>, <a href="/wiki/South_Asia" title="South Asia">South Asia</a>, and part of <a href="/wiki/Western_Asia" title="Western Asia">Western Asia</a>. Written evidence of Indo-European appeared during the Bronze Age in the form of <a href="/wiki/Mycenaean_Greek" title="Mycenaean Greek">Mycenaean Greek</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Anatolian_languages" title="Anatolian languages">Anatolian languages</a> of <a href="/wiki/Hittite_language" title="Hittite language">Hittite</a> and <a href="/wiki/Luwian_language" title="Luwian language">Luwian</a>. The oldest records are isolated Hittite words and names — interspersed in texts that are otherwise in the unrelated <a href="/wiki/Akkadian_language" title="Akkadian language">Akkadian language</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Semitic_languages" title="Semitic languages">Semitic language</a> — found in texts of the <a href="/wiki/Assyria" title="Assyria">Assyrian</a> colony of <a href="/wiki/K%C3%BCltepe" title="Kültepe">Kültepe</a> in eastern <a href="/wiki/Anatolia" title="Anatolia">Anatolia</a> dating to the 20th century BC.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2"><a href="#cite_note-2">[2]</a></sup> Although no older written records of the original <a href="/wiki/Proto-Indo-Europeans" title="Proto-Indo-Europeans">Proto-Indo-European population</a> remain, some aspects of <a href="/wiki/Proto-Indo-European_society" title="Proto-Indo-European society">their culture</a> and <a href="/wiki/Proto-Indo-European_mythology" title="Proto-Indo-European mythology">their religion</a> can be reconstructed from later evidence in the daughter cultures.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3"><a href="#cite_note-3">[3]</a></sup> The Indo-European family is significant to the field of <a href="/wiki/Historical_linguistics" title="Historical linguistics">historical linguistics</a> as it possesses the second-longest <a href="/wiki/Recorded_history" title="Recorded history">recorded history</a> of any known family, after the <a href="/wiki/Afroasiatic_languages" title="Afroasiatic languages">Afroasiatic family</a> in the form of the <a href="/wiki/Egyptian_language" title="Egyptian language">pre-Arab Egyptian language</a> and the Semitic languages. The analysis of the family relationships between the Indo-European languages, and the reconstruction of their common source, was central to the development of the methodology of historical linguistics as an academic discipline in the 19th century.
</p><p>The Indo-European family is not known to be linked to any other language family through any more distant <a href="/wiki/Genetic_relationship_(linguistics)" title="Genetic relationship (linguistics)">genetic relationship</a>, although several <a href="#Proposed_external_relations">disputed proposals</a> to that effect have been made.
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History_of_Indo-European_linguistics">History of Indo-European linguistics</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Indo-European_studies#History" title="Indo-European studies">Indo-European studies § History</a></div>
<p>During the 16th century, European visitors to the <a href="/wiki/Indian_subcontinent" title="Indian subcontinent">Indian subcontinent</a> began to notice similarities among <a href="/wiki/Indo-Aryan_languages" title="Indo-Aryan languages">Indo-Aryan</a>, <a href="/wiki/Iranian_languages" title="Iranian languages">Iranian</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Languages_of_Europe" title="Languages of Europe">European</a> languages. In 1583, English <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Jesuit" title="Jesuit">Jesuit</a> <a href="/wiki/Missionary" title="Missionary">missionary</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Konkani" title="Konkani">Konkani</a> scholar <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Stephens_(Jesuit)" title="Thomas Stephens (Jesuit)">Thomas Stephens</a> wrote a letter from <a href="/wiki/Goa" title="Goa">Goa</a> to his brother (not published until the 20th century)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auroux_4-0"><a href="#cite_note-auroux-4">[4]</a></sup> in which he noted similarities between Indian languages and <a href="/wiki/Greek_language" title="Greek language">Greek</a> and <a href="/wiki/Latin" title="Latin">Latin</a>.
</p><p>Another account was made by <a href="/wiki/Filippo_Sassetti" title="Filippo Sassetti">Filippo Sassetti</a>, a merchant born in <a href="/wiki/Florence" title="Florence">Florence</a> in 1540, who travelled to the Indian subcontinent. Writing in 1585, he noted some word similarities between <a href="/wiki/Sanskrit" title="Sanskrit">Sanskrit</a> and Italian (these included <i>devaḥ</i>/<i>dio</i> "God", <i>sarpaḥ</i>/<i>serpe</i> "serpent", <i>sapta</i>/<i>sette</i> "seven", <i>aṣṭa</i>/<i>otto</i> "eight", and <i>nava</i>/<i>nove</i> "nine").<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auroux_4-1"><a href="#cite_note-auroux-4">[4]</a></sup> However, neither Stephens' nor Sassetti's observations led to further scholarly inquiry.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auroux_4-2"><a href="#cite_note-auroux-4">[4]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1647, <a href="/wiki/Dutch_people" title="Dutch people">Dutch</a> linguist and scholar <a href="/wiki/Marcus_Zuerius_van_Boxhorn" title="Marcus Zuerius van Boxhorn">Marcus Zuerius van Boxhorn</a> noted the similarity among certain Asian and European languages and theorized that they were derived from a primitive common language which he called Scythian.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Scythian_5-0"><a href="#cite_note-Scythian-5">[5]</a></sup> He included in his hypothesis <a href="/wiki/Dutch_language" title="Dutch language">Dutch</a>, <a href="/wiki/Albanian_language" title="Albanian language">Albanian</a>, <a href="/wiki/Greek_language" title="Greek language">Greek</a>, <a href="/wiki/Latin" title="Latin">Latin</a>, <a href="/wiki/Persian_language" title="Persian language">Persian</a>, and <a href="/wiki/German_language" title="German language">German</a>, later adding <a href="/wiki/Slavic_languages" title="Slavic languages">Slavic</a>, <a href="/wiki/Celtic_languages" title="Celtic languages">Celtic</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Baltic_languages" title="Baltic languages">Baltic languages</a>. However, Van Boxhorn's suggestions did not become widely known and did not stimulate further research.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Franz_Bopp_-_Imagines_philologorum.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="471" data-file-width="388" decoding="async" height="206" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b8/Franz_Bopp_-_Imagines_philologorum.jpg/170px-Franz_Bopp_-_Imagines_philologorum.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b8/Franz_Bopp_-_Imagines_philologorum.jpg/255px-Franz_Bopp_-_Imagines_philologorum.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b8/Franz_Bopp_-_Imagines_philologorum.jpg/340px-Franz_Bopp_-_Imagines_philologorum.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Franz_Bopp_-_Imagines_philologorum.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Franz Bopp was a pioneer in the field of comparative linguistic studies.</div></div></div>
<p>Ottoman Turkish traveler <a href="/wiki/Evliya_%C3%87elebi" title="Evliya Çelebi">Evliya Çelebi</a> visited Vienna in 1665–1666 as part of a diplomatic mission and noted a few similarities between words in German and in Persian.
<a href="/wiki/Gaston-Laurent_Coeurdoux" title="Gaston-Laurent Coeurdoux">Gaston Coeurdoux</a> and others made observations of the same type. Coeurdoux made a thorough comparison of Sanskrit, Latin and Greek <a href="/wiki/Grammatical_conjugation" title="Grammatical conjugation">conjugations</a> in the late 1760s to suggest a relationship among them. Meanwhile, <a href="/wiki/Mikhail_Lomonosov" title="Mikhail Lomonosov">Mikhail Lomonosov</a> compared different language groups, including Slavic, Baltic ("<a href="/wiki/Courland" title="Courland">Kurlandic</a>"), Iranian ("<a href="/wiki/Median_language" title="Median language">Medic</a>"), <a href="/wiki/Finnish_language" title="Finnish language">Finnish</a>, <a href="/wiki/Chinese_language" title="Chinese language">Chinese</a>, "Hottentot" (<a href="/wiki/Khoekhoe_language" title="Khoekhoe language">Khoekhoe</a>), and others, noting that related languages (including Latin, Greek, German and Russian) must have separated in antiquity from common ancestors.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lomonosov_6-0"><a href="#cite_note-Lomonosov-6">[6]</a></sup>
</p><p>The hypothesis reappeared in 1786 when <a href="/wiki/William_Jones_(philologist)" title="William Jones (philologist)">Sir William Jones</a> first lectured on the striking similarities among three of the oldest languages known in his time: <a href="/wiki/Latin" title="Latin">Latin</a>, <a href="/wiki/Greek_language" title="Greek language">Greek</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Sanskrit" title="Sanskrit">Sanskrit</a>, to which he tentatively added <a href="/wiki/Gothic_language" title="Gothic language">Gothic</a>, <a href="/wiki/Celtic_languages" title="Celtic languages">Celtic</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Persian_language" title="Persian language">Persian</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup> though his classification contained some inaccuracies and omissions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup> In one of the most famous quotations in linguistics, Jones made the following prescient statement in a lecture to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Asiatic_Society_of_Bengal" title="Asiatic Society of Bengal">Asiatic Society of Bengal</a> in 1786, conjecturing the existence of an earlier ancestor language, which he called "a common source" but did not name:
</p>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r996844942">.mw-parser-output .templatequote{overflow:hidden;margin:1em 0;padding:0 40px}.mw-parser-output .templatequote .templatequotecite{line-height:1.5em;text-align:left;padding-left:1.6em;margin-top:0}</style><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>The Sanscrit  [<i><a href="/wiki/Sic" title="Sic">sic</a></i>] language, whatever be its antiquity, is of a wonderful structure; more perfect than the Greek, more copious than the Latin, and more exquisitely refined than either, yet bearing to both of them a stronger affinity, both in the roots of verbs and the forms of grammar, than could possibly have been produced by accident; so strong indeed, that no philologer could examine them all three, without believing them to have sprung from some common source, which, perhaps, no longer exists.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9"><a href="#cite_note-9">[note 1]</a></sup></p><div class="templatequotecite">— <cite>Sir William Jones, Third Anniversary Discourse delivered 2 February 1786, ELIOHS<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jones-1807_10-0"><a href="#cite_note-Jones-1807-10">[9]</a></sup></cite></div></blockquote>
<p><a href="/wiki/Thomas_Young_(scientist)" title="Thomas Young (scientist)">Thomas Young</a> first used the term <i>Indo-European</i> in 1813, deriving it from the geographical extremes of the language family: from <a href="/wiki/Western_Europe" title="Western Europe">Western Europe</a> to <a href="/wiki/North_India" title="North India">North India</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11"><a href="#cite_note-11">[10]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[11]</a></sup> A synonym is <b>Indo-Germanic</b> (<i>Idg.</i> or <i>IdG.</i>), specifying the family's southeasternmost and northwesternmost branches. This first appeared in French (<i>indo-germanique</i>) in 1810 in the work of <a href="/wiki/Conrad_Malte-Brun" title="Conrad Malte-Brun">Conrad Malte-Brun</a>; in most languages this term is now dated or less common than <i>Indo-European</i>, although in German <i>indogermanisch</i> remains the standard scientific term. A <a href="/wiki/Indo-European_studies#Naming" title="Indo-European studies">number of other synonymous terms</a> have also been used.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Franz_Bopp" title="Franz Bopp">Franz Bopp</a> wrote in 1816 <i>On the conjugational system of the Sanskrit language compared with that of Greek, Latin, Persian and Germanic</i><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[12]</a></sup> and between 1833 and 1852 he wrote <i>Comparative Grammar</i>. This marks the beginning of <a href="/wiki/Indo-European_studies" title="Indo-European studies">Indo-European studies</a> as an academic discipline. The classical phase of Indo-European <a href="/wiki/Comparative_linguistics" title="Comparative linguistics">comparative linguistics</a> leads from this work to <a href="/wiki/August_Schleicher" title="August Schleicher">August Schleicher</a>'s 1861 <i>Compendium</i> and up to <a href="/wiki/Karl_Brugmann" title="Karl Brugmann">Karl Brugmann</a>'s <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Grundriss_der_vergleichenden_Grammatik_der_indogermanischen_Sprachen" title="Grundriss der vergleichenden Grammatik der indogermanischen Sprachen">Grundriss</a></i>, published in the 1880s. Brugmann's <a href="/wiki/Neogrammarian" title="Neogrammarian">neogrammarian</a> reevaluation of the field and <a href="/wiki/Ferdinand_de_Saussure" title="Ferdinand de Saussure">Ferdinand de Saussure</a>'s development of the <a href="/wiki/Laryngeal_theory" title="Laryngeal theory">laryngeal theory</a> may be considered the beginning of "modern" Indo-European studies. The generation of Indo-Europeanists active in the last third of the 20th century (such as <a href="/wiki/Calvert_Watkins" title="Calvert Watkins">Calvert Watkins</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jochem_Schindler" title="Jochem Schindler">Jochem Schindler</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Helmut_Rix" title="Helmut Rix">Helmut Rix</a>) developed a better understanding of morphology and of <a href="/wiki/Indo-European_ablaut" title="Indo-European ablaut">ablaut</a> in the wake of <a href="/wiki/Jerzy_Kury%C5%82owicz" title="Jerzy Kuryłowicz">Kuryłowicz</a>'s 1956 <i>Apophony in Indo-European,</i> who in 1927 pointed out the existence of the <a href="/wiki/Hittite_phonology" title="Hittite phonology">Hittite consonant</a> ḫ.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[13]</a></sup> Kuryłowicz's discovery supported Ferdinand de Saussure's 1879 proposal of the existence of <i>coefficients sonantiques</i>, elements de Saussure reconstructed to account for vowel length alternations in Indo-European languages. This led to the so-called <a href="/wiki/Laryngeal_theory" title="Laryngeal theory">laryngeal theory</a>, a major step forward in Indo-European linguistics and a confirmation of de Saussure's theory.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (May 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Classification">Classification</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Indo-European_migrations" title="Indo-European migrations">Indo-European migrations</a></div>
<p>The various subgroups of the Indo-European language family include ten major branches, listed below in alphabetical order:
</p>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Albanian_language" title="Albanian language">Albanian</a>, attested from the 13th century AD;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dictalit_15-0"><a href="#cite_note-dictalit-15">[14]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Proto-Albanian_language" title="Proto-Albanian language">Proto-Albanian</a> evolved from an ancient <a href="/wiki/Paleo-Balkan_languages" title="Paleo-Balkan languages">Paleo-Balkan language</a>, traditionally thought to be <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Illyrian_languages" title="Illyrian languages">Illyrian</a>, or otherwise a totally unattested Balkan <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Indo-European_language" title="Indo-European language">Indo-European language</a> that was closely related to Illyrian and <a href="/wiki/Messapic_language" title="Messapic language">Messapic</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[15]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-De_Vaan_17-0"><a href="#cite_note-De_Vaan-17">[16]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[17]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Anatolian_languages" title="Anatolian languages">Anatolian</a>, extinct by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Late_Antiquity" title="Late Antiquity">Late Antiquity</a>, spoken in <a href="/wiki/Anatolia" title="Anatolia">Anatolia</a>, attested in isolated terms in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Luwian" title="Luwian">Luwian</a>/<a href="/wiki/Hittites" title="Hittites">Hittite</a> mentioned in <a href="/wiki/Semitic_languages" title="Semitic languages">Semitic</a> <a href="/wiki/Akkadian_language" title="Akkadian language">Old Assyrian</a> texts from the 20th and 19th centuries BC, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hittite_texts" title="Hittite texts">Hittite texts</a> from about 1650 BC.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[18]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[19]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Armenian_language" title="Armenian language">Armenian</a>, attested from the early 5th century AD.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Balto-Slavic_languages" title="Balto-Slavic languages">Balto-Slavic</a>, believed by most Indo-Europeanists<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[20]</a></sup> to form a phylogenetic unit, while a minority ascribes similarities to prolonged language-contact.
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Slavic_languages" title="Slavic languages">Slavic</a> (from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Proto-Slavic" title="Proto-Slavic">Proto-Slavic</a>), attested from the 9th century AD (<a href="/wiki/Pre-Christian_Slavic_writing" title="Pre-Christian Slavic writing">possibly earlier</a>), earliest texts in <a href="/wiki/Old_Church_Slavonic" title="Old Church Slavonic">Old Church Slavonic</a>. Slavic languages include <a href="/wiki/Bulgarian_language" title="Bulgarian language">Bulgarian</a>, <a href="/wiki/Russian_language" title="Russian language">Russian</a>, <a href="/wiki/Polish_language" title="Polish language">Polish</a>, <a href="/wiki/Czech_language" title="Czech language">Czech</a>, <a href="/wiki/Slovak_language" title="Slovak language">Slovak</a>, <a href="/wiki/Silesian_language" title="Silesian language">Silesian</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kashubian_language" title="Kashubian language">Kashubian</a>, <a href="/wiki/Macedonian_language" title="Macedonian language">Macedonian</a>, <a href="/wiki/Serbo-Croatian" title="Serbo-Croatian">Serbo-Croatian</a> (<a href="/wiki/Bosnian_language" title="Bosnian language">Bosnian</a>, <a href="/wiki/Croatian_language" title="Croatian language">Croatian</a>, <a href="/wiki/Montenegrin_language" title="Montenegrin language">Montenegrin</a>, <a href="/wiki/Serbian_language" title="Serbian language">Serbian</a>), <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sorbian_language" title="Sorbian language">Sorbian</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Slovenian_language" title="Slovenian language">Slovenian</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ukrainian_language" title="Ukrainian language">Ukrainian</a>, <a href="/wiki/Belarusian_language" title="Belarusian language">Belarusian</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Rusyn_language" title="Rusyn language">Rusyn</a>.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Baltic_languages" title="Baltic languages">Baltic</a>, attested from the 14th century AD; although attested relatively recently, they retain many archaic features attributed to <a href="/wiki/Proto-Indo-European_language" title="Proto-Indo-European language">Proto-Indo-European</a> (PIE). Living examples are <a href="/wiki/Lithuanian_language" title="Lithuanian language">Lithuanian</a> and <a href="/wiki/Latvian_language" title="Latvian language">Latvian</a>.</li></ul></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Celtic_languages" title="Celtic languages">Celtic</a> (from <a href="/wiki/Proto-Celtic_language" title="Proto-Celtic language">Proto-Celtic</a>), attested since the 6th century BC; <a href="/wiki/Lepontic_language" title="Lepontic language">Lepontic</a> inscriptions date as early as the 6th century BC; <a href="/wiki/Celtiberian_language" title="Celtiberian language">Celtiberian</a> from the 2nd century BC; <a href="/wiki/Primitive_Irish" title="Primitive Irish">Primitive Irish</a> <a href="/wiki/Ogham_inscription" title="Ogham inscription">Ogham inscriptions</a> from the 4th or 5th century AD, earliest inscriptions in <a href="/wiki/Old_Welsh" title="Old Welsh">Old Welsh</a> from the 7th century AD. Modern Celtic languages include <a href="/wiki/Welsh_language" title="Welsh language">Welsh</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cornish_language" title="Cornish language">Cornish</a>, <a href="/wiki/Breton_language" title="Breton language">Breton</a>, <a href="/wiki/Scottish_Gaelic" title="Scottish Gaelic">Scottish Gaelic</a>, <a href="/wiki/Irish_language" title="Irish language">Irish</a> and <a href="/wiki/Manx_language" title="Manx language">Manx</a>.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Germanic_languages" title="Germanic languages">Germanic</a> (from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Proto-Germanic" title="Proto-Germanic">Proto-Germanic</a>), earliest attestations in <a href="/wiki/Runes" title="Runes">runic</a> inscriptions from around the 2nd century AD, earliest coherent texts in <a href="/wiki/Gothic_language" title="Gothic language">Gothic</a>, 4th century AD. <a href="/wiki/Old_English" title="Old English">Old English</a> manuscript tradition from about the 8th century AD. Includes <a href="/wiki/English_language" title="English language">English</a>, <a href="/wiki/Frisian_languages" title="Frisian languages">Frisian</a>, <a href="/wiki/German_language" title="German language">German</a>, <a href="/wiki/Dutch_language" title="Dutch language">Dutch</a>, <a href="/wiki/Scots_language" title="Scots language">Scots</a>, <a href="/wiki/Danish_language" title="Danish language">Danish</a>, <a href="/wiki/Swedish_language" title="Swedish language">Swedish</a>, <a href="/wiki/Norwegian_language" title="Norwegian language">Norwegian</a>, <a href="/wiki/Afrikaans" title="Afrikaans">Afrikaans</a>, <a href="/wiki/Yiddish" title="Yiddish">Yiddish</a>, <a href="/wiki/Low_German" title="Low German">Low German</a>, <a href="/wiki/Icelandic_language" title="Icelandic language">Icelandic</a>, <a href="/wiki/Elfdalian" title="Elfdalian">Elfdalian</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Faroese_language" title="Faroese language">Faroese</a>.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Hellenic_languages" title="Hellenic languages">Hellenic</a> (from <a href="/wiki/Proto-Greek_language" title="Proto-Greek language">Proto-Greek</a>, see also <a href="/wiki/History_of_Greek" title="History of Greek">History of Greek</a>); fragmentary records in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mycenaean_language" title="Mycenaean language">Mycenaean</a> <a href="/wiki/Greek_language" title="Greek language">Greek</a> from between 1450 and 1350 BC have been found.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[21]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Homer" title="Homer">Homeric</a> texts date to the 8th century BC.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Indo-Iranian_languages" title="Indo-Iranian languages">Indo-Iranian</a>, attested circa 1400 BC, descended from <a href="/wiki/Proto-Indo-Iranian_language" title="Proto-Indo-Iranian language">Proto-Indo-Iranian</a> (dated to the late 3rd millennium BC).
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Indo-Aryan_languages" title="Indo-Aryan languages">Indo-Aryan</a>, attested from around 1400 BC in <a href="/wiki/Hittite_language" title="Hittite language">Hittite</a> texts from <a href="/wiki/Anatolia" title="Anatolia">Anatolia</a>, showing <a href="/wiki/Indo-Aryan_superstrate_in_Mitanni" title="Indo-Aryan superstrate in Mitanni">traces of Indo-Aryan</a> words.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[22]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[23]</a></sup> Epigraphically from the 3rd century BC in the form of <a href="/wiki/Prakrit" title="Prakrit">Prakrit</a> (<a href="/wiki/Edicts_of_Ashoka" title="Edicts of Ashoka">Edicts of Ashoka</a>). The <a href="/wiki/Rigveda" title="Rigveda">Rigveda</a> is assumed to preserve intact records <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Patha" title="Patha">via oral tradition</a> dating from about the mid-<a href="/wiki/2nd_millennium_BC" title="2nd millennium BC">second millennium BC</a> in the form of <a href="/wiki/Vedic_Sanskrit" title="Vedic Sanskrit">Vedic Sanskrit</a>. Includes a wide range of modern languages from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Northern_India" title="Northern India">Northern India</a>, Eastern <a href="/wiki/Pakistan" title="Pakistan">Pakistan</a> and <a href="/wiki/Bangladesh" title="Bangladesh">Bangladesh</a>, including <a href="/wiki/Hindustani_language" title="Hindustani language">Hindustani</a> (<a href="/wiki/Hindi" title="Hindi">Hindi</a>, <a href="/wiki/Urdu" title="Urdu">Urdu</a>), <a href="/wiki/Bengali_language" title="Bengali language">Bengali</a>, <a href="/wiki/Odia_language" title="Odia language">Odia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Assamese_language" title="Assamese language">Assamese</a>, <a href="/wiki/Punjabi_language" title="Punjabi language">Punjabi</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kashmiri_language" title="Kashmiri language">Kashmiri</a>, <a href="/wiki/Gujarati_language" title="Gujarati language">Gujarati</a>, <a href="/wiki/Marathi_language" title="Marathi language">Marathi</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sindhi_language" title="Sindhi language">Sindhi</a> and <a href="/wiki/Nepali_language" title="Nepali language">Nepali</a>, as well as <a href="/wiki/Sinhala_language" title="Sinhala language">Sinhala</a> of <a href="/wiki/Sri_Lanka" title="Sri Lanka">Sri Lanka</a> and <a href="/wiki/Maldivian_language" title="Maldivian language">Dhivehi</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Maldives" title="Maldives">Maldives</a> and <a href="/wiki/Minicoy" title="Minicoy">Minicoy</a>.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Iranian_languages" title="Iranian languages">Iranian</a> or Iranic, attested from roughly 1000 BC in the form of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Avestan_language" title="Avestan language">Avestan</a>. Epigraphically from 520 BC in the form of <a href="/wiki/Old_Persian" title="Old Persian">Old Persian</a> (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Behistun_inscription" title="Behistun inscription">Behistun inscription</a>). Includes <a href="/wiki/Persian_language" title="Persian language">Persian</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pashto" title="Pashto">Pashto</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kurdish_languages" title="Kurdish languages">Kurdish</a> <a href="/wiki/Balochi_language" title="Balochi language">Balochi</a>, <a href="/wiki/Luri_language" title="Luri language">Luri</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Ossetian_language" title="Ossetian language">Ossetian</a>.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Nuristani_languages" title="Nuristani languages">Nuristani</a> (includes <a href="/wiki/Kamkata-vari_language" title="Kamkata-vari language">Kamkata-vari</a>, <a href="/wiki/Wasi-wari" title="Wasi-wari">Vasi-vari</a>, <a href="/wiki/Askunu_language" title="Askunu language">Askunu</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Waigali_language" title="Waigali language">Waigali</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tregami_language" title="Tregami language">Tregami</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Zemiaki_language" title="Zemiaki language">Zemiaki</a>).</li></ul></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Italic_languages" title="Italic languages">Italic</a> (from <a href="/wiki/Proto-Italic_language" title="Proto-Italic language">Proto-Italic</a>), attested from the 7th century BC. Includes the ancient <a href="/wiki/Osco-Umbrian_languages" title="Osco-Umbrian languages">Osco-Umbrian languages</a>, <a href="/wiki/Faliscan_language" title="Faliscan language">Faliscan</a>, as well as <a href="/wiki/Latin" title="Latin">Latin</a> and its descendants, the <a href="/wiki/Romance_languages" title="Romance languages">Romance languages</a>, such as <a href="/wiki/Italian_language" title="Italian language">Italian</a>, <a href="/wiki/Venetian_language" title="Venetian language">Venetian</a>, <a href="/wiki/Galician_language" title="Galician language">Galician</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sardinian_language" title="Sardinian language">Sardinian</a>, <a href="/wiki/Neapolitan_language" title="Neapolitan language">Neapolitan</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sicilian_language" title="Sicilian language">Sicilian</a>, <a href="/wiki/Spanish_language" title="Spanish language">Spanish</a>, <a href="/wiki/Asturleonese_language" title="Asturleonese language">Asturleonese</a>, <a href="/wiki/French_language" title="French language">French</a>, <a href="/wiki/Romansh_language" title="Romansh language">Romansh</a>, <a href="/wiki/Occitan_language" title="Occitan language">Occitan</a>, <a href="/wiki/Portuguese_language" title="Portuguese language">Portuguese</a>, <a href="/wiki/Romanian_language" title="Romanian language">Romanian</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Catalan_language" title="Catalan language">Catalan</a>.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Tocharian_languages" title="Tocharian languages">Tocharian</a>, with proposed links to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Afanasevo_culture" title="Afanasevo culture">Afanasevo culture</a> of Southern Siberia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[24]</a></sup> Extant in two dialects (Turfanian and Kuchean, or Tocharian A and B), attested from roughly the 6th to the 9th century AD. Marginalized by the <a href="/wiki/Old_Turkic" title="Old Turkic">Old Turkic</a> <a href="/wiki/Uyghur_Khaganate" title="Uyghur Khaganate">Uyghur Khaganate</a> and probably extinct by the 10th century.</li></ul>
<p>In addition to the classical ten branches listed above, several extinct and little-known languages and language-groups have existed or are proposed to have existed:
</p>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Ancient_Belgian_language" title="Ancient Belgian language">Ancient Belgian</a>: hypothetical language associated with the proposed <a href="/wiki/Nordwestblock" title="Nordwestblock">Nordwestblock</a> cultural area. Speculated to be connected to Italic or Venetic, and to have certain phonological features in common with Lusitanian.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[25]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bernard_27-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bernard-27">[26]</a></sup></li>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cimmerian_language" title="Cimmerian language">Cimmerian</a>: possibly Iranic, Thracian, or Celtic</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Dacian_language" title="Dacian language">Dacian</a>: possibly very close to Thracian</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Elymian_language" title="Elymian language">Elymian</a>: Poorly-attested language spoken by the <a href="/wiki/Elymians" title="Elymians">Elymians</a>, one of the three indigenous (i.e. pre-Greek and pre-Punic) tribes of Sicily. Indo-European affiliation widely accepted, possibly related to Italic or Anatolian.]<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Olga_28-0"><a href="#cite_note-Olga-28">[27]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[28]</a></sup></li>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Illyrian_languages" title="Illyrian languages">Illyrian</a>: possibly related to Albanian, Messapian, or both</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Liburnian_language" title="Liburnian language">Liburnian</a>: evidence too scant and uncertain to determine anything with certainty</li>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ligurian_language_(ancient)" title="Ligurian language (ancient)">Ligurian</a>: possibly close to or part of Celtic.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-kruta1_30-0"><a href="#cite_note-kruta1-30">[29]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Lusitanian_language" title="Lusitanian language">Lusitanian</a>: possibly related to (or part of) Celtic, Ligurian, or Italic</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Ancient_Macedonian_language" title="Ancient Macedonian language">Ancient Macedonian</a>: proposed relationship to Greek.</li>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Messapian_language" title="Messapian language">Messapian</a>: not conclusively deciphered</li>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Paionian_language" title="Paionian language">Paionian</a>: extinct language once spoken north of Macedon</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Phrygian_language" title="Phrygian language">Phrygian</a>: language of the ancient <a href="/wiki/Phrygians" title="Phrygians">Phrygians</a>.  Very likely, but not certainly, a sister group to Hellenic.</li>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sicel_language" title="Sicel language">Sicel</a>: an ancient language spoken by the Sicels (Greek Sikeloi, Latin Siculi), one of the three indigenous (i.e. pre-Greek and pre-Punic) tribes of Sicily. Proposed relationship to Latin or proto-Illyrian (Pre-Indo-European) at an earlier stage.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[30]</a></sup></li>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sorothaptic_language" title="Sorothaptic language">Sorothaptic</a>: proposed, pre-Celtic, Iberian language</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Thracian_language" title="Thracian language">Thracian</a>: possibly including Dacian</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Venetic_language" title="Venetic language">Venetic</a>: shares several similarities with Latin and the Italic languages, but also has some affinities with other IE languages, especially Germanic and Celtic.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[31]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[32]</a></sup></li></ul>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:402px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:IndoEuropeanTree.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1535" data-file-width="1665" decoding="async" height="369" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4f/IndoEuropeanTree.svg/400px-IndoEuropeanTree.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4f/IndoEuropeanTree.svg/600px-IndoEuropeanTree.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4f/IndoEuropeanTree.svg/800px-IndoEuropeanTree.svg.png 2x" width="400"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:IndoEuropeanTree.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Indo-European family tree in order of first attestation</div></div></div><div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:402px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:IndoEuropeanLanguageFamilyRelationsChart.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1200" data-file-width="2000" decoding="async" height="240" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1a/IndoEuropeanLanguageFamilyRelationsChart.jpg/400px-IndoEuropeanLanguageFamilyRelationsChart.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1a/IndoEuropeanLanguageFamilyRelationsChart.jpg/600px-IndoEuropeanLanguageFamilyRelationsChart.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1a/IndoEuropeanLanguageFamilyRelationsChart.jpg/800px-IndoEuropeanLanguageFamilyRelationsChart.jpg 2x" width="400"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:IndoEuropeanLanguageFamilyRelationsChart.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Indo-European language family tree based on "Ancestry-constrained phylogenetic analysis of Indo-European languages" by Chang et al. <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-chang_34-0"><a href="#cite_note-chang-34">[33]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>Membership of languages in the Indo-European language family is determined by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Genetic_(linguistics)" title="Genetic (linguistics)">genealogical</a> relationships, meaning that all members are presumed descendants of a common ancestor, <a href="/wiki/Proto-Indo-European_language" title="Proto-Indo-European language">Proto-Indo-European</a>. Membership in the various branches, groups and subgroups of Indo-European is also genealogical, but here the defining factors are <i>shared innovations</i> among various languages, suggesting a common ancestor that split off from other Indo-European groups. For example, what makes the Germanic languages a branch of Indo-European is that much of their structure and phonology can be stated in rules that apply to all of them. Many of their common features are presumed innovations that took place in <a href="/wiki/Proto-Germanic_language" title="Proto-Germanic language">Proto-Germanic</a>, the source of all the Germanic languages.
</p><p>In the 21st century, several attempts have been made to model the phylogeny of Indo-European languages using Bayesian methodologies similar to those applied to problems in biological phylogeny.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-remco_35-0"><a href="#cite_note-remco-35">[34]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-drinka_36-0"><a href="#cite_note-drinka-36">[35]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-chang_34-1"><a href="#cite_note-chang-34">[33]</a></sup> Although there are differences in absolute timing between the various analyses, there is much commonality between them, including the result that the first known language groups to diverge were the Anatolian and Tocharian language families, in that order.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Tree_versus_wave_model">Tree versus wave model</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Language_change" title="Language change">Language change</a></div>
<p>The "<a href="/wiki/Tree_model" title="Tree model">tree model</a>" is considered an appropriate representation of the genealogical history of a language family if communities do not remain in contact after their languages have started to diverge. In this case, subgroups defined by shared innovations form a nested pattern. The tree model is not appropriate in cases where languages remain in contact as they diversify; in such cases subgroups may overlap, and the "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wave_model_(linguistics)" title="Wave model (linguistics)">wave model</a>" is a more accurate representation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[36]</a></sup> Most approaches to Indo-European subgrouping to date have assumed that the tree model is by-and-large valid for Indo-European;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[37]</a></sup> however, there is also a long tradition of wave-model approaches.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[38]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[39]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPorzig1954_41-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPorzig1954-41">[40]</a></sup>
</p><p>In addition to genealogical changes, many of the early changes in Indo-European languages can be attributed to <a href="/wiki/Language_contact" title="Language contact">language contact</a>. It has been asserted, for example, that many of the more striking features shared by Italic languages (Latin, Oscan, Umbrian, etc.) might well be <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Areal_feature_(linguistics)" title="Areal feature (linguistics)">areal features</a>. More certainly, very similar-looking alterations in the systems of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Long_vowel" title="Long vowel">long vowels</a> in the West Germanic languages greatly postdate any possible notion of a <a href="/wiki/Proto-language" title="Proto-language">proto-language</a> innovation (and cannot readily be regarded as "areal", either, because English and continental West Germanic were not a linguistic area). In a similar vein, there are many similar innovations in Germanic and Balto-Slavic that are far more likely areal features than traceable to a common proto-language, such as the uniform development of a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/High_vowel" title="High vowel">high vowel</a> (*<i>u</i> in the case of Germanic, *<i>i/u</i> in the case of Baltic and Slavic) before the PIE syllabic resonants *<i>ṛ, *ḷ, *ṃ, *ṇ</i>, unique to these two groups among IE languages, which is in agreement with the wave model. The <a href="/wiki/Balkan_sprachbund" title="Balkan sprachbund">Balkan sprachbund</a> even features areal convergence among members of very different branches.
</p><p>An extension to the <i><a href="/wiki/Donald_Ringe" title="Donald Ringe">Ringe</a>-<a href="/wiki/Tandy_Warnow" title="Tandy Warnow">Warnow</a> model of language evolution</i>, suggests that early IE had featured limited contact between distinct lineages, with only the Germanic subfamily exhibiting a less treelike behaviour as it acquired some characteristics from neighbours early in its evolution. The internal diversification of especially West Germanic is cited to have been radically non-treelike.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[41]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Proposed_subgroupings">Proposed subgroupings</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1045330069" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><table class="sidebar nomobile nowraplinks hlist" style="width:22em"><tbody><tr><th class="sidebar-title" style="font-size: 1.25em;">Hypothetical <a class="mw-selflink selflink">Indo-European</a><br/>phylogenetic clades</th></tr><tr><th class="sidebar-heading">
Balkan</th></tr><tr><td class="sidebar-content">
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Paleo-Balkan_languages" title="Paleo-Balkan languages">Paleo-Balkan</a></li>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Daco-Thracian" title="Daco-Thracian">Daco-Thracian</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Graeco-Armenian" title="Graeco-Armenian">Graeco-Armenian</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Graeco-Aryan" title="Graeco-Aryan">Graeco-Aryan</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Graeco-Phrygian" title="Graeco-Phrygian">Graeco-Phrygian</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Armeno-Phrygian_languages" title="Armeno-Phrygian languages">Armeno-Phrygian</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Thraco-Illyrian" title="Thraco-Illyrian">Thraco-Illyrian</a></li></ul></td>
</tr><tr><th class="sidebar-heading">
Other</th></tr><tr><td class="sidebar-content">
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Italo-Celtic" title="Italo-Celtic">Italo-Celtic</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Indo-Hittite" title="Indo-Hittite">Indo-Hittite</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Indo-Uralic_languages" title="Indo-Uralic languages">Indo-Uralic</a></li></ul></td>
</tr><tr><td class="sidebar-navbar"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1063604349" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="navbar plainlinks hlist navbar-mini"><ul><li class="nv-view"><a href="/wiki/Template:Hypothetical_Indo-European_subfamilies" title="Template:Hypothetical Indo-European subfamilies"><abbr title="View this template">v</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-talk"><a href="/wiki/Template_talk:Hypothetical_Indo-European_subfamilies" title="Template talk:Hypothetical Indo-European subfamilies"><abbr title="Discuss this template">t</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-edit"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:Hypothetical_Indo-European_subfamilies&amp;action=edit"><abbr title="Edit this template">e</abbr></a></li></ul></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>Specialists have postulated the existence of higher-order subgroups such as <a href="/wiki/Italo-Celtic" title="Italo-Celtic">Italo-Celtic</a>, <a href="/wiki/Graeco-Armenian" title="Graeco-Armenian">Graeco-Armenian</a>, <a href="/wiki/Graeco-Aryan" title="Graeco-Aryan">Graeco-Aryan</a> or Graeco-Armeno-Aryan, and Balto-Slavo-Germanic. However, unlike the ten traditional branches, these are all controversial to a greater or lesser degree.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[42]</a></sup>
</p><p>The Italo-Celtic subgroup was at one point uncontroversial, considered by <a href="/wiki/Antoine_Meillet" title="Antoine Meillet">Antoine Meillet</a> to be even better established than Balto-Slavic.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPorzig195439_44-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPorzig195439-44">[43]</a></sup> The main lines of evidence included the genitive suffix <i>-ī</i>; the superlative suffix <i>-m̥mo</i>; the change of /p/ to /kʷ/ before another /kʷ/ in the same word (as in <i>penkʷe</i> &gt; <i>*kʷenkʷe</i> &gt; Latin <i>quīnque</i>, Old Irish <i>cóic</i>); and the subjunctive morpheme <i>-ā-</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFortson2004247_45-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFortson2004247-45">[44]</a></sup> This evidence was prominently challenged by <a href="/wiki/Calvert_Watkins" title="Calvert Watkins">Calvert Watkins</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[45]</a></sup> while Michael Weiss has argued for the subgroup.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[46]</a></sup>
</p><p>Evidence for a relationship between Greek and Armenian includes the regular change of the <a href="/wiki/Laryngeal_theory" title="Laryngeal theory">second laryngeal</a> to <i>a</i> at the beginnings of words, as well as terms for "woman" and "sheep".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[47]</a></sup> Greek and Indo-Iranian share innovations mainly in verbal morphology and patterns of nominal derivation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[48]</a></sup> Relations have also been proposed between Phrygian and Greek,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[49]</a></sup> and between Thracian and Armenian.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[50]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[51]</a></sup> Some fundamental shared features, like the <a href="/wiki/Aorist" title="Aorist">aorist</a> (a verb form denoting action without reference to duration or completion) having the perfect active particle -s fixed to the stem, link this group closer to Anatolian languages<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[52]</a></sup> and Tocharian. Shared features with Balto-Slavic languages, on the other hand (especially present and preterit formations), might be due to later contacts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[53]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Indo-Hittite" title="Indo-Hittite">Indo-Hittite</a> hypothesis proposes that the Indo-European language family consists of two main branches: one represented by the Anatolian languages and another branch encompassing all other Indo-European languages. Features that separate Anatolian from all other branches of Indo-European (such as the gender or the verb system) have been interpreted alternately as archaic debris or as innovations due to prolonged isolation. Points proffered in favour of the Indo-Hittite hypothesis are the (non-universal) Indo-European agricultural terminology in Anatolia<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[54]</a></sup> and the preservation of laryngeals.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[55]</a></sup> However, in general this hypothesis is considered to attribute too much weight to the Anatolian evidence. According to another view, the Anatolian subgroup left the Indo-European parent language comparatively late, approximately at the same time as Indo-Iranian and later than the Greek or Armenian divisions. A third view, especially prevalent in the so-called French school of Indo-European studies, holds that extant similarities in non-<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Satem" title="Satem">satem</a> languages in general—including Anatolian—might be due to their peripheral location in the Indo-European language-area and to early separation, rather than indicating a special ancestral relationship.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[56]</a></sup> Hans J. Holm, based on lexical calculations, arrives at a picture roughly replicating the general scholarly opinion and refuting the Indo-Hittite hypothesis.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[57]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Satem_and_centum_languages">Satem and centum languages</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Centum_and_satem_languages" title="Centum and satem languages">Centum and satem languages</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:352px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Indo-European_isoglosses.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="517" data-file-width="845" decoding="async" height="214" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/05/Indo-European_isoglosses.png/350px-Indo-European_isoglosses.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/05/Indo-European_isoglosses.png/525px-Indo-European_isoglosses.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/05/Indo-European_isoglosses.png/700px-Indo-European_isoglosses.png 2x" width="350"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Indo-European_isoglosses.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Some significant isoglosses in Indo-European daughter languages at around 500 BC. <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r981673959" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="legend"><span class="legend-color mw-no-invert" style="background-color:#9fc7f3; color:black;"> </span> Blue: centum languages</div> <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r981673959" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="legend"><span class="legend-color mw-no-invert" style="background-color:#ef7a6e; color:black;"> </span> Red: satem languages</div> <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r981673959" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="legend"><span class="legend-color mw-no-invert" style="background-color:#f6a20f; color:black;"> </span> Orange: languages with <a href="/wiki/Augment_(Indo-European)" title="Augment (Indo-European)">augment</a></div> <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r981673959" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="legend"><span class="legend-color mw-no-invert" style="background-color:#a1f091; color:black;"> </span> Green: languages with PIE *-tt- &gt; -ss-</div> <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r981673959" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="legend"><span class="legend-color mw-no-invert" style="background-color:#f6d3ab; color:black;"> </span> Tan: languages with PIE *-tt- &gt; -st-</div> <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r981673959" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="legend"><span class="legend-color mw-no-invert" style="background-color:#fdd1d1; color:black;"> </span> Pink: languages with instrumental, dative and ablative plural endings (and some others) in *-m- rather than *-bh-</div></div></div></div>
<p>The division of the Indo-European languages into satem and centum groups was put forward by Peter von Bradke in 1890, although <a href="/wiki/Karl_Brugmann" title="Karl Brugmann">Karl Brugmann</a> did propose a similar type of division in 1886. In the satem languages, which include the Balto-Slavic and Indo-Iranian branches, as well as (in most respects) Albanian and Armenian, the reconstructed <a href="/wiki/Proto-Indo-European_phonology#Consonants" title="Proto-Indo-European phonology">Proto-Indo-European palatovelars</a> remained distinct and were fricativized, while the labiovelars merged with the 'plain velars'. In the centum languages, the palatovelars merged with the plain velars, while the labiovelars remained distinct. The results of these alternative developments are exemplified by the words for "hundred" in Avestan (<i>satem</i>) and Latin (<i>centum</i>)—the initial palatovelar developed into a fricative <span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[s]</span> in the former, but became an ordinary velar <span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[k]</span> in the latter.
</p><p>Rather than being a genealogical separation, the centum–satem division is commonly seen as resulting from innovative changes that spread across PIE dialect-branches over a particular geographical area; the centum–satem <a href="/wiki/Isogloss" title="Isogloss">isogloss</a> intersects a number of other isoglosses that mark distinctions between features in the early IE branches. It may be that the centum branches in fact reflect the original state of affairs in PIE, and only the satem branches shared a set of innovations, which affected all but the peripheral areas of the PIE dialect continuum.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[58]</a></sup> Kortlandt proposes that the ancestors of Balts and Slavs took part in satemization before being drawn later into the western Indo-European sphere.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[59]</a></sup>
</p>

Unsourced material may be challenged and removed.</span> <span class="date-container"><i>(<span class="date">June 2021</span>)</i></span><span class="hide-when-compact"><i> (<small><a href="/wiki/Help:Maintenance_template_removal" title="Help:Maintenance template removal">Learn how and when to remove this template message</a></small>)</i></span></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>From the very beginning of Indo-European studies, there have been attempts to link the Indo-European languages genealogically to other languages and language families. However, these theories remain highly controversial, and most specialists in Indo-European linguistics are sceptical or agnostic about such proposals.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kallio2018_61-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kallio2018-61">[60]</a></sup>
</p><p>Proposals linking the Indo-European languages with a single language family include:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kallio2018_61-1"><a href="#cite_note-Kallio2018-61">[60]</a></sup>
</p>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Indo-Uralic_languages" title="Indo-Uralic languages">Indo-Uralic</a>, joining Indo-European with <a href="/wiki/Uralic_languages" title="Uralic languages">Uralic</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Pontic_languages" title="Pontic languages">Pontic</a>, postulated by <a href="/wiki/John_Colarusso" title="John Colarusso">John Colarusso</a>, which joins Indo-European with <a href="/wiki/Northwest_Caucasian_languages" title="Northwest Caucasian languages">Northwest Caucasian</a></li></ul>
<p>Other proposed families include:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kallio2018_61-2"><a href="#cite_note-Kallio2018-61">[60]</a></sup>
</p>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Nostratic_languages" title="Nostratic languages">Nostratic</a>, comprising all or some of the Eurasiatic languages and the <a href="/wiki/Kartvelian_languages" title="Kartvelian languages">Kartvelian</a>, <a href="/wiki/Dravidian_languages" title="Dravidian languages">Dravidian</a> (or wider, <a href="/wiki/Elamo-Dravidian_languages" title="Elamo-Dravidian languages">Elamo-Dravidian</a>) and <a href="/wiki/Afroasiatic_languages" title="Afroasiatic languages">Afroasiatic</a> language families</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Eurasiatic_languages" title="Eurasiatic languages">Eurasiatic</a>, a theory championed by <a href="/wiki/Joseph_Greenberg" title="Joseph Greenberg">Joseph Greenberg</a>, comprising the <a href="/wiki/Uralic_languages" title="Uralic languages">Uralic</a>, <a href="/wiki/Altaic_languages" title="Altaic languages">Altaic</a> and various '<a href="/wiki/Paleosiberian_languages" title="Paleosiberian languages">Paleosiberian</a>' families (<a href="/wiki/Ainu_languages" title="Ainu languages">Ainu</a>, <a href="/wiki/Yukaghir_languages" title="Yukaghir languages">Yukaghir</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nivkh_languages" title="Nivkh languages">Nivkh</a>, <a href="/wiki/Chukotko-Kamchatkan_languages" title="Chukotko-Kamchatkan languages">Chukotko-Kamchatkan</a>, <a href="/wiki/Eskimo%E2%80%93Aleut_languages" title="Eskimo–Aleut languages">Eskimo–Aleut</a>) and possibly others</li></ul>
<p>Nostratic and Eurasiatic, in turn, have been included in even wider groupings, such as <a href="/wiki/Borean_languages" title="Borean languages">Borean</a>, a language family separately proposed by <a href="/wiki/Harold_C._Fleming" title="Harold C. Fleming">Harold C. Fleming</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sergei_Starostin" title="Sergei Starostin">Sergei Starostin</a> that encompasses almost all of the world's natural languages with the exception of those native to <a href="/wiki/Sub-Saharan_Africa" title="Sub-Saharan Africa">sub-Saharan Africa</a>, <a href="/wiki/New_Guinea" title="New Guinea">New Guinea</a>, <a href="/wiki/Australia" title="Australia">Australia</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Andaman_Islands" title="Andaman Islands">Andaman Islands</a>.
</p><p>Objections to such groupings are not based on any theoretical claim about the likely historical existence or nonexistence of such <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Macrofamilies" title="Macrofamilies">macrofamilies</a>; it is entirely reasonable to suppose that they might have existed. The serious difficulty lies in identifying the details of actual relationships between language families, because it is very hard to find concrete evidence that transcends chance resemblance or is not equally likely explained as being due to <a href="/wiki/Loanword" title="Loanword">borrowing</a>, including <i><a href="/wiki/Wanderwort" title="Wanderwort">Wanderwörter</a></i>, which can travel very long distances. Because the <a href="/wiki/Signal-to-noise_ratio" title="Signal-to-noise ratio">signal-to-noise ratio</a> in historical linguistics declines over time, at great enough time-depths it becomes open to reasonable doubt that one can even distinguish between signal and noise.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Evolution">Evolution</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Proto-Indo-European">Proto-Indo-European</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Proto-Indo-European_language" title="Proto-Indo-European language">Proto-Indo-European language</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Indo-European_expansions.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1024" data-file-width="2094" decoding="async" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a6/Indo-European_expansions.jpg/300px-Indo-European_expansions.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a6/Indo-European_expansions.jpg/450px-Indo-European_expansions.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a6/Indo-European_expansions.jpg/600px-Indo-European_expansions.jpg 2x" width="300"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Indo-European_expansions.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Scheme of Indo-European language dispersals from c. 4000 to 1000 BCE according to the widely held <a href="/wiki/Kurgan_hypothesis" title="Kurgan hypothesis">Kurgan hypothesis</a>.<br/>– Center: Steppe cultures<br/>1 (black): Anatolian languages (archaic PIE)<br/>2 (black): Afanasievo culture (early PIE)<br/>3 (black) Yamnaya culture expansion (Pontic-Caspian steppe, Danube Valley) (late PIE)<br/>4A (black): Western Corded Ware<br/>4B-C (blue &amp; dark blue): Bell Beaker; adopted by Indo-European speakers<br/>5A-B (red): Eastern Corded ware<br/>5C (red): Sintashta (proto-Indo-Iranian)<br/>6 (magenta): Andronovo<br/>7A (purple): Indo-Aryans (Mittani)<br/>7B (purple): Indo-Aryans (India)<br/>[NN] (dark yellow): proto-Balto-Slavic<br/>8 (grey): Greek<br/>9 (yellow):Iranians<br/>– [not drawn]: Armenian, expanding from western steppe</div></div></div>
<p>The proposed Proto-Indo-European language (PIE) is the <a href="/wiki/Comparative_method" title="Comparative method">reconstructed</a> common ancestor of the Indo-European languages, spoken by the <a href="/wiki/Proto-Indo-Europeans" title="Proto-Indo-Europeans">Proto-Indo-Europeans</a>. From the 1960s, knowledge of Anatolian became certain enough to establish its relationship to PIE. Using the method of <a href="/wiki/Internal_reconstruction" title="Internal reconstruction">internal reconstruction</a>, an earlier stage, called Pre-Proto-Indo-European, has been proposed.
</p><p>PIE was an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Inflected_language" title="Inflected language">inflected language</a>, in which the grammatical relationships between words were signaled through inflectional morphemes (usually endings). The <a href="/wiki/Root_(linguistics)" title="Root (linguistics)">roots</a> of PIE are basic <a href="/wiki/Morpheme" title="Morpheme">morphemes</a> carrying a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lexical_(semiotics)" title="Lexical (semiotics)">lexical</a> meaning. By addition of <a href="/wiki/Suffix" title="Suffix">suffixes</a>, they form <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Stem_(linguistics)" title="Stem (linguistics)">stems</a>, and by addition of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ending_(linguistics)" title="Ending (linguistics)">endings</a>, these form grammatically inflected words (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Indo-European_noun" title="Indo-European noun">nouns</a> or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Indo-European_verb" title="Indo-European verb">verbs</a>). The reconstructed <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Indo-European_verb" title="Indo-European verb">Indo-European verb</a> system is complex and, like the noun, exhibits a system of <a href="/wiki/Indo-European_ablaut" title="Indo-European ablaut">ablaut</a>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Diversification">Diversification</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Indo-European_migrations" title="Indo-European migrations">Indo-European migrations</a></div>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1021810730">@media all and (max-width:720px){.mw-parser-output .mod-gallery{width:100%!important}}.mw-parser-output .mod-gallery{display:table}.mw-parser-output .mod-gallery-default{background:transparent;margin-top:0.3em}.mw-parser-output .mod-gallery-center{margin-left:auto;margin-right:auto}.mw-parser-output .mod-gallery-left{float:left}.mw-parser-output .mod-gallery-right{float:right}.mw-parser-output .mod-gallery-none{float:none}.mw-parser-output .mod-gallery-collapsible{width:100%}.mw-parser-output .mod-gallery .title,.mw-parser-output .mod-gallery .main,.mw-parser-output .mod-gallery .footer{display:table-row}.mw-parser-output .mod-gallery .title>div{display:table-cell;padding:0.2em 0 0.6em 1.6em;text-align:center;font-weight:bold}.mw-parser-output .mod-gallery .main>div{display:table-cell}.mw-parser-output .mod-gallery .gallery{line-height:1.35em}.mw-parser-output .mod-gallery .footer>div{display:table-cell;padding:0.2em 0 0.6em 1.6em;text-align:right;font-size:80%;line-height:1em}.mw-parser-output .mod-gallery .title>div *,.mw-parser-output .mod-gallery .footer>div *{overflow:visible}.mw-parser-output .mod-gallery .gallerybox img{background:none!important}.mw-parser-output .mod-gallery .bordered-images .thumb img{border:solid #eaecf0 1px}.mw-parser-output .mod-gallery .whitebg .thumb{background:#fff!important}</style><div class="mod-gallery mod-gallery-default mod-gallery-center"><div class="title"><div>Possible expansion of Indo-European languages according to the Kurgan hypothesis</div></div><div class="main"><div><ul class="gallery mw-gallery-traditional nochecker bordered-images whitebg">

</div></li>
<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 215px"><div style="width: 215px">
<div class="thumb" style="width: 210px;"><div style="margin:24px auto;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:IE3500BP.png" title="IE languages c. 1500 BC"><img alt="IE languages 1500 BC" data-file-height="169" data-file-width="370" decoding="async" height="82" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/36/IE3500BP.png/180px-IE3500BP.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/36/IE3500BP.png/270px-IE3500BP.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/36/IE3500BP.png/360px-IE3500BP.png 2x" width="180"/></a></div></div>

</p>
</div>
</div></li>
<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 215px"><div style="width: 215px">
<div class="thumb" style="width: 210px;"><div style="margin:24px auto;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:IE1500BP.png" title="IE languages c. 500 AD"><img alt="IE languages 500 AD" data-file-height="169" data-file-width="370" decoding="async" height="82" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9f/IE1500BP.png/180px-IE1500BP.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9f/IE1500BP.png/270px-IE1500BP.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9f/IE1500BP.png/360px-IE1500BP.png 2x" width="180"/></a></div></div>
