</p>
</div>
</div></li>
</ul></div></div></div>
<p>BMAC in "IE languages c. 1500 BC" is <a href="/wiki/Bactria%E2%80%93Margiana_Archaeological_Complex" title="Bactria–Margiana Archaeological Complex">Bactria–Margiana Archaeological Complex</a>
</p><p><br/>
The diversification of the parent language into the attested branches of daughter languages is historically unattested. The timeline of the evolution of the various daughter languages, on the other hand, is mostly undisputed, quite regardless of the question of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Indo-European_origins" title="Indo-European origins">Indo-European origins</a>.
</p><p>Using a mathematical analysis borrowed from evolutionary biology, <a href="/wiki/Donald_Ringe" title="Donald Ringe">Donald Ringe</a> and <a href="/wiki/Tandy_Warnow" title="Tandy Warnow">Tandy Warnow</a> propose the following evolutionary tree of Indo-European branches:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAnthony200756–58_62-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAnthony200756–58-62">[61]</a></sup>
</p>
<ul><li>Pre-<a href="/wiki/Anatolian_languages" title="Anatolian languages">Anatolian</a> (before 3500 BC)</li>
<li>Pre-<a href="/wiki/Tocharian_languages" title="Tocharian languages">Tocharian</a></li>
<li>Pre-Italic and Pre-Celtic (before 2500 BC)</li>
<li>Pre-Armenian and Pre-Greek (after 2500 BC)</li>
<li>Proto-<a href="/wiki/Indo-Iranian_languages" title="Indo-Iranian languages">Indo-Iranian</a> (2000 BC)</li>
<li>Pre-Germanic and Pre-Balto-Slavic;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAnthony200756–58_62-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAnthony200756–58-62">[61]</a></sup> proto-Germanic c. 500 BC<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERinge200667_63-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERinge200667-63">[62]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wikipedia:CITESHORT" title="Wikipedia:CITESHORT"><span title="More information is required to link this short citation to its long citation. (July 2021)">incomplete short citation</span></a></i>]</sup></li></ul>
<p>David Anthony proposes the following sequence:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAnthony2007100_64-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAnthony2007100-64">[63]</a></sup>
</p>
<ul><li>Pre-<a href="/wiki/Anatolian_languages" title="Anatolian languages">Anatolian</a> (4200 BC)</li>
<li>Pre-<a href="/wiki/Tocharian_languages" title="Tocharian languages">Tocharian</a> (3700 BC)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Germanic_parent_language" title="Germanic parent language">Pre-Germanic</a> (3300 BC)</li>
<li>Pre-Italic and Pre-Celtic (3000 BC)</li>
<li>Pre-Armenian (2800 BC)</li>
<li>Pre-Balto-Slavic (2800 BC)</li>
<li>Pre-Greek (2500 BC)</li>
<li>Proto-<a href="/wiki/Indo-Iranian_languages" title="Indo-Iranian languages">Indo-Iranian</a> (2200 BC); split between Iranian and Old Indic 1800 BC</li></ul>
<p>From 1500 BC the following sequence may be given:<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (November 2019)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p>
<ul><li>1500–1000 BC: The <a href="/wiki/Nordic_Bronze_Age" title="Nordic Bronze Age">Nordic Bronze Age</a> develops <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pre-Proto-Germanic" title="Pre-Proto-Germanic">pre-Proto-Germanic</a>, and the (pre)-<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Proto-Celtic" title="Proto-Celtic">Proto-Celtic</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Urnfield" title="Urnfield">Urnfield</a> and <a href="/wiki/Hallstatt_culture" title="Hallstatt culture">Hallstatt</a> cultures emerge in Central Europe, introducing the <a href="/wiki/Iron_Age" title="Iron Age">Iron Age</a>. Migration of the Proto-<a href="/wiki/Italic_languages" title="Italic languages">Italic</a> speakers into the Italian peninsula (<a href="/wiki/Bagnolo_stele" title="Bagnolo stele">Bagnolo stele</a>). Redaction of the <a href="/wiki/Rigveda" title="Rigveda">Rigveda</a> and rise of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Vedic_civilization" title="Vedic civilization">Vedic civilization</a> in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Punjab_region" title="Punjab region">Punjab</a>. The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mycenaean_civilization" title="Mycenaean civilization">Mycenaean civilization</a> gives way to the <a href="/wiki/Greek_Dark_Ages" title="Greek Dark Ages">Greek Dark Ages</a>. Hittite goes extinct.</li>
<li>1000–500 BC: The <a href="/wiki/Celtic_languages" title="Celtic languages">Celtic languages</a> spread over Central and Western Europe. <a href="/wiki/Baltic_languages" title="Baltic languages">Baltic languages</a> are spoken in a huge area from present-day Poland to the Ural Mountains.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[64]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Proto_Germanic" title="Proto Germanic">Proto Germanic</a>. <a href="/wiki/Homer" title="Homer">Homer</a> and the beginning of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Classical_Antiquity" title="Classical Antiquity">Classical Antiquity</a>. The Vedic Civilization gives way to the <a href="/wiki/Mahajanapadas" title="Mahajanapadas">Mahajanapadas</a>. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Siddhartha_Gautama" title="Siddhartha Gautama">Siddhartha Gautama</a> preaches <a href="/wiki/Buddhism" title="Buddhism">Buddhism</a>. <a href="/wiki/Zoroaster" title="Zoroaster">Zoroaster</a> composes the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gathas" title="Gathas">Gathas</a>, rise of the <a href="/wiki/Achaemenid_Empire" title="Achaemenid Empire">Achaemenid Empire</a>, replacing the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Elamites" title="Elamites">Elamites</a> and <a href="/wiki/Babylonia" title="Babylonia">Babylonia</a>. Separation of Proto-Italic into <a href="/wiki/Osco-Umbrian_languages" title="Osco-Umbrian languages">Osco-Umbrian</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Latin-Faliscan_languages" title="Latin-Faliscan languages">Latin-Faliscan</a>. Genesis of the <a href="/wiki/Greek_alphabet" title="Greek alphabet">Greek</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Old_Italic_alphabet" title="Old Italic alphabet">Old Italic</a> alphabets. A variety of <a href="/wiki/Paleo-Balkan_languages" title="Paleo-Balkan languages">Paleo-Balkan languages</a> are spoken in Southern Europe.</li>
<li>500 BC – 1 BC/AD: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Classical_Antiquity" title="Classical Antiquity">Classical Antiquity</a>: spread of <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greek" title="Ancient Greek">Greek</a> and <a href="/wiki/Latin" title="Latin">Latin</a> throughout the Mediterranean and, during the <a href="/wiki/Hellenistic_period" title="Hellenistic period">Hellenistic period</a> (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Indo-Greeks" title="Indo-Greeks">Indo-Greeks</a>), to Central Asia and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hindukush" title="Hindukush">Hindukush</a>. <a href="/wiki/Kushan_Empire" title="Kushan Empire">Kushan Empire</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mauryan_Empire" title="Mauryan Empire">Mauryan Empire</a>. <a href="/wiki/Proto-Germanic_language" title="Proto-Germanic language">Proto-Germanic</a>.</li>
<li>1 BC – AD 500: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Late_Antiquity" title="Late Antiquity">Late Antiquity</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gupta_period" title="Gupta period">Gupta period</a>; attestation of <a href="/wiki/Armenian_language" title="Armenian language">Armenian</a>. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Proto-Slavic" title="Proto-Slavic">Proto-Slavic</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Roman_Empire" title="Roman Empire">Roman Empire</a> and then the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Migration_period" title="Migration period">Migration period</a> marginalize the Celtic languages to the British Isles. <a href="/wiki/Sogdian_language" title="Sogdian language">Sogdian</a>, an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Eastern_Iranian_language" title="Eastern Iranian language">Eastern Iranian language</a>, becomes the <i><a href="/wiki/Lingua_franca" title="Lingua franca">lingua franca</a></i> of the <a href="/wiki/Silk_Road" title="Silk Road">Silk Road</a> in Central Asia leading to China, due to the proliferation of <a href="/wiki/Sogdia" title="Sogdia">Sogdian</a> merchants there. The last of the Anatolian languages are <a href="/wiki/Language_death" title="Language death">extinct</a>.</li>
<li>500–1000: <a href="/wiki/Early_Middle_Ages" title="Early Middle Ages">Early Middle Ages</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Viking_Age" title="Viking Age">Viking Age</a> forms an <a href="/wiki/Old_Norse" title="Old Norse">Old Norse</a> <a href="/wiki/Koin%C3%A9_language" title="Koiné language">koine</a> spanning Scandinavia, the British Isles and Iceland. The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Islamic_conquest" title="Islamic conquest">Islamic conquest</a> and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Turkic_expansion" title="Turkic expansion">Turkic expansion</a> results in the <a href="/wiki/Arabization" title="Arabization">Arabization</a> and <a href="/wiki/Turkification" title="Turkification">Turkification</a> of significant areas where Indo-European languages were spoken. <a href="/wiki/Tocharian_languages" title="Tocharian languages">Tocharian</a> is extinct in the course of the Turkic expansion while <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Northeastern_Iranian_languages" title="Northeastern Iranian languages">Northeastern Iranian</a> (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Scytho-Sarmatian" title="Scytho-Sarmatian">Scytho-Sarmatian</a>) is reduced to small refugia. Slavic languages spread over wide areas in central, eastern and southeastern Europe, largely replacing Romance in the Balkans (with the exception of Romanian) and whatever was left of the <a href="/wiki/Paleo-Balkan_languages" title="Paleo-Balkan languages">paleo-Balkan languages</a> with the exception of Albanian.</li>
<li>1000–1500: <a href="/wiki/Late_Middle_Ages" title="Late Middle Ages">Late Middle Ages</a>: Attestation of <a href="/wiki/Albanian_language" title="Albanian language">Albanian</a> and <a href="/wiki/Baltic_languages" title="Baltic languages">Baltic</a>.</li>
<li>1500–2000: <a href="/wiki/Early_modern_Europe" title="Early modern Europe">Early Modern period</a> to present: <a href="/wiki/Colonialism" title="Colonialism">Colonialism</a> results in the spread of Indo-European languages to every continent, most notably <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Romance_language" title="Romance language">Romance</a> (North, Central and South America, North and Sub-Saharan Africa, West Asia), <a href="/wiki/West_Germanic_languages" title="West Germanic languages">West Germanic</a> (<a href="/wiki/English_language" title="English language">English</a> in North America, Sub-Saharan Africa, East Asia and Australia; to a lesser extent Dutch and German), and <a href="/wiki/Russian_language" title="Russian language">Russian</a> to Central Asia and North Asia.</li></ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Important_languages_for_reconstruction">Important languages for reconstruction</span></h3>
<p>In reconstructing the history of the Indo-European languages and the form of the <a href="/wiki/Proto-Indo-European_language" title="Proto-Indo-European language">Proto-Indo-European language</a>, some languages have been of particular importance. These generally include the ancient Indo-European languages that are both well-attested and documented at an early date, although some languages from later periods are important if they are particularly <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Conservative_(language)" title="Conservative (language)">linguistically conservative</a> (most notably, <a href="/wiki/Lithuanian_language" title="Lithuanian language">Lithuanian</a>). Early poetry is of special significance because of the rigid <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Poetic_meter" title="Poetic meter">poetic meter</a> normally employed, which makes it possible to reconstruct a number of features (e.g. <a href="/wiki/Vowel_length" title="Vowel length">vowel length</a>) that were either unwritten or corrupted in the process of transmission down to the earliest extant written <a href="/wiki/Manuscript" title="Manuscript">manuscripts</a>.
</p><p>Most noticeable of all:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Beekes2011One_66-0"><a href="#cite_note-Beekes2011One-66">[65]</a></sup>
</p>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Vedic_Sanskrit" title="Vedic Sanskrit">Vedic Sanskrit</a> (c. 1500–500 BC). This language is unique in that its source documents were all composed orally, and were passed down through <a href="/wiki/Oral_tradition" title="Oral tradition">oral tradition</a> (<a href="/wiki/Shakha" title="Shakha">shakha</a> schools) for c. 2,000 years before ever being written down. The oldest documents are all in poetic form; oldest and most important of all is the <a href="/wiki/Rigveda" title="Rigveda">Rigveda</a> (c. 1500 BC).</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greek" title="Ancient Greek">Ancient Greek</a> (c. 750–400 BC). <a href="/wiki/Mycenaean_Greek" title="Mycenaean Greek">Mycenaean Greek</a> (c. 1450 BC) is the oldest recorded form, but its value is lessened by the limited material, restricted subject matter, and highly ambiguous writing system. More important is Ancient Greek, documented extensively beginning with the two <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Homeric_poems" title="Homeric poems">Homeric poems</a> (the <i><a href="/wiki/Iliad" title="Iliad">Iliad</a></i> and the <i><a href="/wiki/Odyssey" title="Odyssey">Odyssey</a></i>, c. 750 BC).</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Hittite_language" title="Hittite language">Hittite</a> (c. 1700–1200 BC). This is the earliest-recorded of all Indo-European languages, and highly divergent from the others due to the early separation of the <a href="/wiki/Anatolian_languages" title="Anatolian languages">Anatolian languages</a> from the remainder. It possesses some highly archaic features found only fragmentarily, if at all, in other languages. At the same time, however, it appears to have undergone many early phonological and grammatical changes which, combined with the ambiguities of its writing system, hinder its usefulness somewhat.</li></ul>
<p>Other primary sources:
</p>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Latin" title="Latin">Latin</a>, attested in a huge amount of poetic and prose material in the <a href="/wiki/Classical_Latin" title="Classical Latin">Classical</a> period (c. 200 BC – 100 AD) and limited <a href="/wiki/Old_Latin" title="Old Latin">older material</a> from as early as c. 600 BC.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Gothic_language" title="Gothic language">Gothic</a> (the most archaic well-documented <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Germanic_language" title="Germanic language">Germanic language</a>, c. 350 AD), along with the combined witness of the other old Germanic languages: most importantly, <a href="/wiki/Old_English" title="Old English">Old English</a> (c. 800–1000 AD), <a href="/wiki/Old_High_German" title="Old High German">Old High German</a> (c. 750–1000 AD) and <a href="/wiki/Old_Norse" title="Old Norse">Old Norse</a> (c. 1100–1300 AD, with limited earlier sources dating to c. 200 AD).</li>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Old_Avestan" title="Old Avestan">Old Avestan</a> (c. 1700–1200 BC) and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Avestan_language" title="Avestan language">Younger Avestan</a> (c. 900 BC). Documentation is sparse, but nonetheless quite important due to its highly archaic nature.</li>
<li>Modern <a href="/wiki/Lithuanian_language" title="Lithuanian language">Lithuanian</a>, with limited records in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Old_Lithuanian" title="Old Lithuanian">Old Lithuanian</a> (c. 1500–1700 AD).</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Old_Church_Slavonic" title="Old Church Slavonic">Old Church Slavonic</a> (c. 900–1000 AD).</li></ul>
<p>Other secondary sources, of lesser value due to poor attestation:
</p>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Luwian_language" title="Luwian language">Luwian</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lycian_language" title="Lycian language">Lycian</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lydian_language" title="Lydian language">Lydian</a> and other <a href="/wiki/Anatolian_languages" title="Anatolian languages">Anatolian languages</a> (c. 1400–400 BC).</li>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Oscan" title="Oscan">Oscan</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Umbrian" title="Umbrian">Umbrian</a> and other <a href="/wiki/Italic_languages" title="Italic languages">Old Italic</a> languages (c. 600–200 BC).</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Old_Persian" title="Old Persian">Old Persian</a> (c. 500 BC).</li>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Old_Prussian" title="Old Prussian">Old Prussian</a> (c. 1350–1600 AD); even more archaic than Lithuanian.</li></ul>
<p>Other secondary sources, of lesser value due to extensive phonological changes and relatively limited attestation:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Beekes2011_67-0"><a href="#cite_note-Beekes2011-67">[66]</a></sup>
</p>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Old_Irish" title="Old Irish">Old Irish</a> (c. 700–850 AD).</li>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tocharian_language" title="Tocharian language">Tocharian</a> (c. 500–800 AD), underwent large phonetic shifts and mergers in the proto-language, and has an almost entirely reworked declension system.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Classical_Armenian" title="Classical Armenian">Classical Armenian</a> (c. 400–1000 AD).</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Albanian_language" title="Albanian language">Albanian</a> (c. 1450–current time).</li></ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Sound_changes">Sound changes</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Indo-European_sound_laws" title="Indo-European sound laws">Indo-European sound laws</a></div>
<p>As the Proto-Indo-European (PIE) language broke up, its sound system diverged as well, changing according to various <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sound_law" title="Sound law">sound laws</a> evidenced in the <a href="/wiki/Daughter_language" title="Daughter language">daughter languages</a>.
</p><p>PIE is normally reconstructed with a complex system of 15 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Stop_consonant" title="Stop consonant">stop consonants</a>, including an unusual three-way <a href="/wiki/Phonation" title="Phonation">phonation</a> (<a href="/wiki/Voice_(phonetics)" title="Voice (phonetics)">voicing</a>) distinction between <a href="/wiki/Voicelessness" title="Voicelessness">voiceless</a>, <a href="/wiki/Voice_(phonetics)" title="Voice (phonetics)">voiced</a> and "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Voiced_aspirated" title="Voiced aspirated">voiced aspirated</a>" (i.e. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Breathy_voiced" title="Breathy voiced">breathy voiced</a>) stops, and a three-way distinction among <a href="/wiki/Velar_consonant" title="Velar consonant">velar consonants</a> (<i>k</i>-type sounds) between "palatal" <i>ḱ ǵ ǵh</i>, "plain velar" <i>k g gh</i> and <a href="/wiki/Labialized_velar_consonant" title="Labialized velar consonant">labiovelar</a> <i>kʷ gʷ gʷh</i>. (The correctness of the terms <i>palatal</i> and <i>plain velar</i> is disputed; see <a href="/wiki/Proto-Indo-European_phonology" title="Proto-Indo-European phonology">Proto-Indo-European phonology</a>.) All daughter languages have reduced the number of distinctions among these sounds, often in divergent ways.
</p><p>As an example, in <a href="/wiki/English_language" title="English language">English</a>, one of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Germanic_language" title="Germanic language">Germanic languages</a>, the following are some of the major changes that happened:
</p>
<div><ol><li>As in other <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Centum" title="Centum">centum</a> languages, the "plain velar" and "palatal" stops merged, reducing the number of stops from 15 to 12.</li><li>As in the other Germanic languages, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Germanic_sound_shift" title="Germanic sound shift">Germanic sound shift</a> changed the realization of all stop consonants, with each consonant shifting to a different one:
<dl><dd><span class="Unicode" style="white-space:nowrap;" title="Proto-Indo-European language"><span lang="ine">bʰ</span></span> → <span class="Unicode" style="white-space:nowrap;" title="Proto-Indo-European language"><span lang="ine">b</span></span> → <span class="Unicode" style="white-space:nowrap;" title="Proto-Indo-European language"><span lang="ine">p</span></span> → <span class="Unicode" style="white-space:nowrap;" title="Proto-Indo-European language"><span lang="ine">f</span></span></dd>
<dd><span class="Unicode" style="white-space:nowrap;" title="Proto-Indo-European language"><span lang="ine">dʰ</span></span> → <span class="Unicode" style="white-space:nowrap;" title="Proto-Indo-European language"><span lang="ine">d</span></span> → <span class="Unicode" style="white-space:nowrap;" title="Proto-Indo-European language"><span lang="ine">t</span></span> → <span class="Unicode" style="white-space:nowrap;" title="Proto-Indo-European language"><span lang="ine">θ</span></span></dd>
<dd><span class="Unicode" style="white-space:nowrap;" title="Proto-Indo-European language"><span lang="ine">gʰ</span></span> → <span class="Unicode" style="white-space:nowrap;" title="Proto-Indo-European language"><span lang="ine">g</span></span> → <span class="Unicode" style="white-space:nowrap;" title="Proto-Indo-European language"><span lang="ine">k</span></span> → <span class="Unicode" style="white-space:nowrap;" title="Proto-Indo-European language"><span lang="ine">x</span></span> (Later initial <span class="Unicode" style="white-space:nowrap;" title="Proto-Indo-European language"><span lang="ine">x</span></span> →<span class="Unicode" style="white-space:nowrap;" title="Proto-Indo-European language"><span lang="ine">h</span></span>)</dd>
<dd><span class="Unicode" style="white-space:nowrap;" title="Proto-Indo-European language"><span lang="ine">gʷʰ</span></span> → <span class="Unicode" style="white-space:nowrap;" title="Proto-Indo-European language"><span lang="ine">gʷ</span></span> → <span class="Unicode" style="white-space:nowrap;" title="Proto-Indo-European language"><span lang="ine">kʷ</span></span> → <span class="Unicode" style="white-space:nowrap;" title="Proto-Indo-European language"><span lang="ine">xʷ</span></span> (Later initial <span class="Unicode" style="white-space:nowrap;" title="Proto-Indo-European language"><span lang="ine">xʷ</span></span> →<span class="Unicode" style="white-space:nowrap;" title="Proto-Indo-European language"><span lang="ine">hʷ</span></span>)</dd></dl>
<p>Each original consonant shifted one position to the right.  For example, original <span class="Unicode" style="white-space:nowrap;" title="Proto-Indo-European language"><span lang="ine">dʰ</span></span> became <span class="Unicode" style="white-space:nowrap;" title="Proto-Indo-European language"><span lang="ine">d</span></span>, while original <span class="Unicode" style="white-space:nowrap;" title="Proto-Indo-European language"><span lang="ine">d</span></span> became <span class="Unicode" style="white-space:nowrap;" title="Proto-Indo-European language"><span lang="ine">t</span></span> and original <span class="Unicode" style="white-space:nowrap;" title="Proto-Indo-European language"><span lang="ine">t</span></span> became <span class="Unicode" style="white-space:nowrap;" title="Proto-Indo-European language"><span lang="ine">θ</span></span> (written <i>th</i> in English).  This is the original source of the English sounds written <i>f</i>, <i>th</i>, <i>h</i> and <i>wh</i>.  Examples, comparing English with Latin, where the sounds largely remain unshifted:
</p>
<dl><dd>For PIE <i>p</i>: <i>piscis</i> vs. <i>fish</i>; <i>pēs, pēdis</i> vs. <i>foot</i>; <i>pluvium</i> "rain" vs. <i>flow</i>; <i>pater</i> vs. <i>father</i></dd>
<dd>For PIE <i>t</i>: <i>trēs</i> vs. <i>three</i>; <i>māter</i> vs. <i>mother</i></dd>
<dd>For PIE <i>d</i>: <i>decem</i> vs. <i>ten</i>; <i>pēdis</i> vs. <i>foot</i>; <i>quid</i> vs. <i>what</i></dd>
<dd>For PIE <i>k</i>: <i>centum</i> vs. <i>hund(red)</i>; <i>capere</i> "to take" vs. <i>have</i></dd>
<dd>For PIE <i>kʷ</i>: <i>quid</i> vs. <i>what</i>; <i>quandō</i> vs. <i>when</i></dd></dl></li><li>Various further changes affected consonants in the middle or end of a word:
<ul><li>The voiced stops resulting from the sound shift were softened to voiced <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fricative_consonant" title="Fricative consonant">fricatives</a> (or perhaps the sound shift directly generated fricatives in these positions).</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Verner%27s_law" title="Verner's law">Verner's law</a> also turned some of the voiceless fricatives resulting from the sound shift into voiced fricatives or stops.  This is why the <i>t</i> in Latin <i>centum</i> ends up as <i>d</i> in <i>hund(red)</i> rather than the expected <i>th</i>.</li>
<li>Most remaining <i>h</i> sounds disappeared, while remaining <i>f</i> and <i>th</i> became voiced.  For example, Latin <i>decem</i> ends up as <i>ten</i> with no <i>h</i> in the middle (but note <i>taíhun</i> "ten" in <a href="/wiki/Gothic_language" title="Gothic language">Gothic</a>, an archaic Germanic language).  Similarly, the words <i>seven</i> and <i>have</i> have a voiced <i>v</i> (compare Latin <i>septem</i>, <i>capere</i>), while <i>father</i> and <i>mother</i> have a voiced <i>th</i>, although not spelled differently (compare Latin <i>pater</i>, <i>māter</i>).</li></ul></li></ol></div>
<p>None of the daughter-language families (except possibly <a href="/wiki/Anatolian_languages" title="Anatolian languages">Anatolian</a>, particularly <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Luvian_language" title="Luvian language">Luvian</a>) reflect the plain velar stops differently from the other two series, and there is even a certain amount of dispute whether this series existed at all in PIE. The major distinction between <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Centum-satem_isogloss" title="Centum-satem isogloss"><i>centum</i> and <i>satem</i></a> languages corresponds to the outcome of the PIE plain velars:
</p>
<ul><li>The "central" <i>satem</i> languages (<a href="/wiki/Indo-Iranian_languages" title="Indo-Iranian languages">Indo-Iranian</a>, <a href="/wiki/Balto-Slavic_languages" title="Balto-Slavic languages">Balto-Slavic</a>, <a href="/wiki/Albanian_language" title="Albanian language">Albanian</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Armenian_language" title="Armenian language">Armenian</a>) reflect both "plain velar" and labiovelar stops as plain velars, often with secondary <a href="/wiki/Palatalization_(sound_change)" title="Palatalization (sound change)">palatalization</a> before a <a href="/wiki/Front_vowel" title="Front vowel">front vowel</a> (<i>e i ē ī</i>). The "palatal" stops are palatalized and often appear as <a href="/wiki/Sibilant" title="Sibilant">sibilants</a> (usually but not always distinct from the secondarily palatalized stops).</li>
<li>The "peripheral" <i>centum</i> languages (<a href="/wiki/Germanic_languages" title="Germanic languages">Germanic</a>, <a href="/wiki/Italic_languages" title="Italic languages">Italic</a>, <a href="/wiki/Celtic_languages" title="Celtic languages">Celtic</a>, <a href="/wiki/Greek_language" title="Greek language">Greek</a>, <a href="/wiki/Anatolian_languages" title="Anatolian languages">Anatolian</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tocharian_language" title="Tocharian language">Tocharian</a>) reflect both "palatal" and "plain velar" stops as plain velars, while the labiovelars continue unchanged, often with later reduction into plain <a href="/wiki/Labial_consonant" title="Labial consonant">labial</a> or <a href="/wiki/Velar_consonant" title="Velar consonant">velar consonants</a>.</li></ul>
<p>The three-way PIE distinction between voiceless, voiced and voiced aspirated stops is considered extremely unusual from the perspective of <a href="/wiki/Linguistic_typology" title="Linguistic typology">linguistic typology</a>—particularly in the existence of voiced aspirated stops without a corresponding series of voiceless aspirated stops. None of the various daughter-language families continue it unchanged, with numerous "solutions" to the apparently unstable PIE situation:
</p>
<ul><li>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Indo-Aryan_language" title="Indo-Aryan language">Indo-Aryan languages</a> preserve the three series unchanged but have evolved a fourth series of voiceless aspirated consonants.</li>
<li>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Iranian_language" title="Iranian language">Iranian languages</a> probably passed through the same stage, subsequently changing the aspirated stops into fricatives.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Greek_language" title="Greek language">Greek</a> converted the voiced aspirates into voiceless aspirates.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Italic_languages" title="Italic languages">Italic</a> probably passed through the same stage, but reflects the voiced aspirates as voiceless fricatives, especially <i>f</i> (or sometimes plain voiced stops in <a href="/wiki/Latin" title="Latin">Latin</a>).</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Celtic_languages" title="Celtic languages">Celtic</a>, <a href="/wiki/Balto-Slavic_languages" title="Balto-Slavic languages">Balto-Slavic</a>, <a href="/wiki/Anatolian_languages" title="Anatolian languages">Anatolian</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Albanian_language" title="Albanian language">Albanian</a> merge the voiced aspirated into plain voiced stops.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Germanic_languages" title="Germanic languages">Germanic</a> and <a href="/wiki/Armenian_language" title="Armenian language">Armenian</a> change all three series in a <a href="/wiki/Chain_shift" title="Chain shift">chain shift</a> (e.g. with <i>bh b p</i> becoming <i>b p f</i> (known as <i><a href="/wiki/Grimm%27s_law" title="Grimm's law">Grimm's law</a></i> in Germanic)).</li></ul>
<p>Among the other notable changes affecting consonants are:
</p>
<ul><li>The <a href="/wiki/Ruki_sound_law" title="Ruki sound law">Ruki sound law</a> (<i>s</i> becomes <span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/ʃ/</span> before <i>r, u, k, i</i>) in the <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Satem" title="Satem">satem</a></i> languages.</li>
<li>Loss of prevocalic <i>p</i> in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Proto-Celtic" title="Proto-Celtic">Proto-Celtic</a>.</li>
<li>Development of prevocalic <i>s</i> to <i>h</i> in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Proto-Greek" title="Proto-Greek">Proto-Greek</a>, with later loss of <i>h</i> between vowels.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Verner%27s_law" title="Verner's law">Verner's law</a> in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Proto-Germanic" title="Proto-Germanic">Proto-Germanic</a>.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Grassmann%27s_law" title="Grassmann's law">Grassmann's law</a> (dissimilation of aspirates) independently in Proto-Greek and Proto-Indo-Iranian.</li></ul>
<p>The following table shows the basic outcomes of PIE consonants in some of the most important daughter languages for the purposes of reconstruction. For a fuller table, see <a href="/wiki/Indo-European_sound_laws" title="Indo-European sound laws">Indo-European sound laws</a>.
</p>

<td><b><span class="Unicode" style="white-space:nowrap;" title="Proto-Indo-European language"><span lang="ine">v</span></span></b> <span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[ʋ]</span>
</td>

</td>
<td><b><span class="Unicode" style="white-space:nowrap;" title="Proto-Indo-European language"><span lang="ine">u</span></span></b> <span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[w &gt; v]</span>
</td>
<td><b><span class="Unicode" style="white-space:nowrap;" title="Proto-Indo-European language"><span lang="ine">f</span></span></b>;<br/>-<b><span class="Unicode" style="white-space:nowrap;" title="Proto-Indo-European language"><span lang="ine">Ø</span></span></b>-

<th>PIE</th>

<th><a href="/wiki/Gothic_language" title="Gothic language">Gothic</a></th>
<th>English
</th></tr></tbody></table>
<dl><dd>Notes:</dd></dl>
<ul><li><b>C</b>- At the beginning of a word.</li>
<li>-<b>C</b>- Between vowels.</li>
<li>-<b>C</b> At the end of a word.</li>
<li>`-<b>C</b>- Following an unstressed vowel (<a href="/wiki/Verner%27s_law" title="Verner's law">Verner's law</a>).</li>
<li>-<b>C</b>-<sup>(rl)</sup> Between vowels, or between a vowel and <b><span class="Unicode" style="white-space:nowrap;" title="Proto-Indo-European language"><span lang="ine">r, l</span></span></b> (on either side).</li>
<li><b>C</b><sup>T</sup> Before a (PIE) stop (<b><span class="Unicode" style="white-space:nowrap;" title="Proto-Indo-European language"><span lang="ine">p, t, k</span></span></b>).</li>
<li><b>C</b><sup>T−</sup> After a (PIE) obstruent (<b><span class="Unicode" style="white-space:nowrap;" title="Proto-Indo-European language"><span lang="ine">p, t, k</span></span></b>, etc.; <b><span class="Unicode" style="white-space:nowrap;" title="Proto-Indo-European language"><span lang="ine">s</span></span></b>).</li>
<li><b>C</b><sup>(T)</sup> Before or after an obstruent (<b><span class="Unicode" style="white-space:nowrap;" title="Proto-Indo-European language"><span lang="ine">p, t, k</span></span></b>, etc.; <b><span class="Unicode" style="white-space:nowrap;" title="Proto-Indo-European language"><span lang="ine">s</span></span></b>).</li>
<li><b>C</b><sup>H</sup> Before an original laryngeal.</li>
<li><b>C</b><sup>E</sup> Before a (PIE) front vowel (<b><span class="Unicode" style="white-space:nowrap;" title="Proto-Indo-European language"><span lang="ine">i, e</span></span></b>).</li>
<li><b>C</b><sup>E'</sup> Before secondary (post-PIE) front-vowels.</li>
<li><b>C</b><sup>e</sup> Before <b><span class="Unicode" style="white-space:nowrap;" title="Proto-Indo-European language"><span lang="ine">e</span></span></b>.</li>
<li><b>C</b><sup>(u)</sup> Before or after a (PIE) <b><span class="Unicode" style="white-space:nowrap;" title="Proto-Indo-European language"><span lang="ine">u</span></span></b> (<a href="/wiki/Bouk%C3%B3los_rule" title="Boukólos rule">boukólos rule</a>).</li>
<li><b>C</b><sup>(O)</sup> Before or after a (PIE) <b><span class="Unicode" style="white-space:nowrap;" title="Proto-Indo-European language"><span lang="ine">o, u</span></span></b> (<a href="/wiki/Bouk%C3%B3los_rule" title="Boukólos rule">boukólos rule</a>).</li>
<li><b>C</b><sup>n−</sup> After <b><span class="Unicode" style="white-space:nowrap;" title="Proto-Indo-European language"><span lang="ine">n</span></span></b>.</li>
<li><b>C</b><sup>R</sup> Before a <a href="/wiki/Sonorant" title="Sonorant">sonorant</a> (<b><span class="Unicode" style="white-space:nowrap;" title="Proto-Indo-European language"><span lang="ine">r, l, m, n</span></span></b>).</li>
<li><b>C</b><sup>(R)</sup> Before or after a <a href="/wiki/Sonorant" title="Sonorant">sonorant</a> (<b><span class="Unicode" style="white-space:nowrap;" title="Proto-Indo-European language"><span lang="ine">r, l, m, n</span></span></b>).</li>
<li><b>C</b><sup>(r),l,u−</sup> Before <b><span class="Unicode" style="white-space:nowrap;" title="Proto-Indo-European language"><span lang="ine">r, l</span></span></b> or after <b><span class="Unicode" style="white-space:nowrap;" title="Proto-Indo-European language"><span lang="ine">r, u</span></span></b>.</li>
<li><b>C</b><sup>ruki−</sup> After <b><span class="Unicode" style="white-space:nowrap;" title="Proto-Indo-European language"><span lang="ine">r, u, k, i</span></span></b> (<a href="/wiki/Ruki_sound_law" title="Ruki sound law">Ruki sound law</a>).</li>
<li><b>C</b><sup>..Ch</sup> Before an aspirated consonant in the next syllable (<a href="/wiki/Grassmann%27s_law" title="Grassmann's law">Grassmann's law</a>, also known as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dissimilation_of_aspirates" title="Dissimilation of aspirates">dissimilation of aspirates</a>).</li>
<li><b>C</b><sup>E..Ch</sup> Before a (PIE) front vowel (<b><span class="Unicode" style="white-space:nowrap;" title="Proto-Indo-European language"><span lang="ine">i, e</span></span></b>) as well as before an aspirated consonant in the next syllable (<a href="/wiki/Grassmann%27s_law" title="Grassmann's law">Grassmann's law</a>, also known as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dissimilation_of_aspirates" title="Dissimilation of aspirates">dissimilation of aspirates</a>).</li>
<li><b>C</b><sup>(u)..Ch</sup> Before or after a (PIE) <b><span class="Unicode" style="white-space:nowrap;" title="Proto-Indo-European language"><span lang="ine">u</span></span></b> as well as before an aspirated consonant in the next syllable (<a href="/wiki/Grassmann%27s_law" title="Grassmann's law">Grassmann's law</a>, also known as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dissimilation_of_aspirates" title="Dissimilation of aspirates">dissimilation of aspirates</a>).</li></ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Comparison_of_conjugations">Comparison of conjugations</span></h3>
<p>The following table presents a comparison of conjugations of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Vowel_stems" title="Vowel stems">thematic</a> <a href="/wiki/Present_tense" title="Present tense">present indicative</a> of the verbal root *<span class="Unicode" style="white-space:nowrap;" title="Proto-Indo-European language"><span lang="ine">bʰer-</span></span> of the English verb <i><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/bear" title="wikt:bear">to bear</a></i> and its reflexes in various early attested IE languages and their modern descendants or relatives, showing that all languages had in the early stage an inflectional verb system.
</p>

<p>While similarities are still visible between the modern descendants and relatives of these ancient languages, the differences have increased over time. Some IE languages have moved from <a href="/wiki/Synthetic_language" title="Synthetic language">synthetic</a> verb systems to largely <a href="/wiki/Periphrasis" title="Periphrasis">periphrastic</a> systems. In addition, the <a href="/wiki/Pronoun" title="Pronoun">pronouns</a> of periphrastic forms are in parentheses when they appear. Some of these verbs have undergone a change in meaning as well.
</p>
<ul><li>In <a href="/wiki/Irish_language" title="Irish language">Modern Irish</a> <i>beir</i> usually only carries the meaning <i>to bear</i> in the sense of bearing a child; its common meanings are <i>to catch, grab</i>. Apart from the first person, the forms given in the table above are dialectical or obsolete. The second and third person forms are typically instead conjugated <a href="/wiki/Periphrasis" title="Periphrasis">periphrastically</a> by adding a pronoun after the verb: <i>beireann tú, beireann sé/sí, beireann sibh, beireann siad</i>.</li>
<li>The <a href="/wiki/Hindustani_grammar" title="Hindustani grammar">Hindustani</a> (<a href="/wiki/Hindi" title="Hindi">Hindi</a> and <a href="/wiki/Urdu" title="Urdu">Urdu</a>) verb <i>bʰarnā</i>, the continuation of the Sanskrit verb, can have a variety of meanings, but the most common is "to fill". The forms given in the table, although etymologically derived from the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Present_indicative" title="Present indicative">present indicative</a>, now have the meaning of <a href="/wiki/Subjunctive_mood" title="Subjunctive mood">future subjunctive</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_68-0"><a href="#cite_note-:1-68">[67]</a></sup> The loss of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Present_indicative" title="Present indicative">present indicative</a> in Hindustani is roughly compensated by the periphrastic <a href="/wiki/Habitual_aspect" title="Habitual aspect">habitual indicative</a> construction, using the <a href="/wiki/Habitual_aspect" title="Habitual aspect">habitual participle</a> (etymologically from the Sanskrit present participle <i>bʰarant-</i>) and an auxiliary: <i>ma͠i bʰartā hū̃, tū bʰartā hai, vah bʰartā hai, ham bʰarte ha͠i, tum bʰarte ho, ve bʰarte ha͠i</i> (masculine forms).</li>
<li>German is not directly descended from Gothic, but the Gothic forms are a close approximation of what the early West Germanic forms of c. 400 AD would have looked like. The descendant of Proto-Germanic <i>*beraną</i> (English <i>bear</i>) survives in German only in the compound <i>gebären</i>, meaning "bear (a child)".</li>
<li>The Latin verb <i>ferre</i> is irregular, and not a good representative of a normal thematic verb. In most Romance languages such as Portuguese, other verbs now mean "to carry" (e.g. Pt. <i>portar</i> &lt; Lat. <i>portare</i>) and <i>ferre</i> was borrowed and nativized only in compounds such as <span title="Portuguese-language text"><i lang="pt">sofrer</i></span> "to suffer" (from Latin <i>sub-</i> and <i>ferre</i>) and <span title="Portuguese-language text"><i lang="pt">conferir</i></span> "to confer" (from Latin "con-" and "ferre").</li>
<li>In Modern <a href="/wiki/Greek_language" title="Greek language">Greek</a>, <i>phero</i> φέρω (modern transliteration <i>fero</i>) "to bear" is still used but only in specific contexts and is most common in such compounds as αναφέρω, διαφέρω, εισφέρω, εκφέρω, καταφέρω, προφέρω, προαναφέρω, προσφέρω etc. The form that is (very) common today is <i>pherno</i> φέρνω (modern transliteration <i>ferno</i>) meaning "to bring". Additionally, the perfective form of <i>pherno</i> (used for the subjunctive voice and also for the future tense) is also <i>phero</i>.</li>
<li>The dual forms are archaic in standard Lithuanian, and are only presently used in some dialects (e.g. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Samogitian_dialect" title="Samogitian dialect">Samogitian</a>).</li>
<li>Among modern Slavic languages, only Slovene continues to have a dual number in the standard variety.</li></ul>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Comparison_of_cognates">Comparison of cognates</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Indo-European_vocabulary" title="Indo-European vocabulary">Indo-European vocabulary</a></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Proto-Indo-European_numerals" title="Proto-Indo-European numerals">Proto-Indo-European numerals</a> and <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=List_of_numbers_in_various_languages&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="List of numbers in various languages (page does not exist)">List of numbers in various languages</a></div>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Present_distribution">Present distribution</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:342px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Indo-European_distribution.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1398" data-file-width="2754" decoding="async" height="173" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/53/Indo-European_distribution.png/340px-Indo-European_distribution.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/53/Indo-European_distribution.png/510px-Indo-European_distribution.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/53/Indo-European_distribution.png/680px-Indo-European_distribution.png 2x" width="340"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Indo-European_distribution.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r981673959" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="legend"><span class="legend-color mw-no-invert" style="background-color:#0026ff; color:white;"> </span> Countries where Indo-European language family is majority native</div> <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r981673959" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="legend"><span class="legend-color mw-no-invert" style="background-color:#0094ff; color:black;"> </span> Countries where Indo-European language family is official but not majority native</div> <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r981673959" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="legend"><span class="legend-color mw-no-invert" style="background-color:#c0c0c0; color:black;"> </span> Countries where Indo-European language family is not official</div></div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:212px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Americaslanguages_(orthographic_projection)-2.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2000" data-file-width="2008" decoding="async" height="209" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/56/Americaslanguages_%28orthographic_projection%29-2.png/210px-Americaslanguages_%28orthographic_projection%29-2.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/56/Americaslanguages_%28orthographic_projection%29-2.png/315px-Americaslanguages_%28orthographic_projection%29-2.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/56/Americaslanguages_%28orthographic_projection%29-2.png/420px-Americaslanguages_%28orthographic_projection%29-2.png 2x" width="210"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Americaslanguages_(orthographic_projection)-2.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The approximate present-day distribution of Indo-European languages within the Americas by country:<br/> <a href="/wiki/Romance_languages" title="Romance languages">Romance</a>: <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r981673959" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="legend"><span class="legend-color mw-no-invert" style="background-color:#41BB00; color:black;"> </span> <a href="/wiki/Spanish_language" title="Spanish language">Spanish</a></div> <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r981673959" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="legend"><span class="legend-color mw-no-invert" style="background-color:#ED6900; color:black;"> </span> <a href="/wiki/Portuguese_language" title="Portuguese language">Portuguese</a></div> <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r981673959" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="legend"><span class="legend-color mw-no-invert" style="background-color:#001E96; color:white;"> </span> <a href="/wiki/French_language" title="French language">French</a></div> <a href="/wiki/Germanic_languages" title="Germanic languages">Germanic</a>: <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r981673959" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="legend"><span class="legend-color mw-no-invert" style="background-color:#C70000; color:white;"> </span> <a href="/wiki/English_language" title="English language">English</a></div> <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r981673959" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="legend"><span class="legend-color mw-no-invert" style="background-color:#FFD400; color:black;"> </span> <a href="/wiki/Dutch_language" title="Dutch language">Dutch</a></div></div></div></div>
<p>Today, Indo-European languages are spoken by billions of <a class="mw-redirect" href='/wiki/Native_speaker#Defining_"native_speaker"' title="Native speaker">native speakers</a> across all inhabited continents,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[68]</a></sup> the largest number by far for any recognised language family. Of the <a href="/wiki/List_of_languages_by_total_number_of_speakers" title="List of languages by total number of speakers">20 languages with the largest numbers of speakers</a> according to <i>Ethnologue</i>, 10 are Indo-European: <a href="/wiki/English_language" title="English language">English</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hindustani_language" title="Hindustani language">Hindustani</a>, <a href="/wiki/Spanish_language" title="Spanish language">Spanish</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bengali_language" title="Bengali language">Bengali</a>, <a href="/wiki/French_language" title="French language">French</a>, <a href="/wiki/Russian_language" title="Russian language">Russian</a>, <a href="/wiki/Portuguese_language" title="Portuguese language">Portuguese</a>, <a href="/wiki/German_language" title="German language">German</a>, <a href="/wiki/Persian_language" title="Persian language">Persian</a> and <a href="/wiki/Punjabi_language" title="Punjabi language">Punjabi</a>, each with 100 million speakers or more.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[69]</a></sup> Additionally, hundreds of millions of persons worldwide study Indo-European languages as secondary or tertiary languages, including in cultures which have completely different language families and historical backgrounds—there are between 600 million<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-etheng_71-0"><a href="#cite_note-etheng-71">[70]</a></sup> and one billion<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bcouncil_72-0"><a href="#cite_note-bcouncil-72">[71]</a></sup> L2 learners of English alone.
</p><p>The success of the language family, including the large number of speakers and the vast portions of the Earth that they inhabit, is due to several factors. The ancient <a href="/wiki/Indo-European_migrations" title="Indo-European migrations">Indo-European migrations</a> and widespread dissemination of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Indo-European_culture" title="Indo-European culture">Indo-European culture</a> throughout <a href="/wiki/Eurasia" title="Eurasia">Eurasia</a>, including that of the <a href="/wiki/Proto-Indo-Europeans" title="Proto-Indo-Europeans">Proto-Indo-Europeans</a> themselves, and that of their daughter cultures including the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Indo-Aryan_migration_theory" title="Indo-Aryan migration theory">Indo-Aryans</a>, <a href="/wiki/Iranian_peoples" title="Iranian peoples">Iranian peoples</a>, <a href="/wiki/Celts" title="Celts">Celts</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hellenistic_period" title="Hellenistic period">Greeks</a>, <a href="/wiki/Roman_Empire" title="Roman Empire">Romans</a>, <a href="/wiki/Germanic_peoples" title="Germanic peoples">Germanic peoples</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Slavs" title="Slavs">Slavs</a>, led to these peoples' branches of the language family already taking a dominant foothold in virtually all of <a href="/wiki/Eurasia" title="Eurasia">Eurasia</a> except for swathes of the <a href="/wiki/Near_East" title="Near East">Near East</a>, <a href="/wiki/North_Asia" title="North Asia">North</a> and <a href="/wiki/East_Asia" title="East Asia">East Asia</a>, replacing many (but not all) of the previously-spoken <a href="/wiki/Pre-Indo-European_languages" title="Pre-Indo-European languages">pre-Indo-European languages</a> of this extensive area. However <a href="/wiki/Semitic_languages" title="Semitic languages">Semitic languages</a> remain dominant in much of the <a href="/wiki/Middle_East" title="Middle East">Middle East</a> and <a href="/wiki/North_Africa" title="North Africa">North Africa</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Languages_of_the_Caucasus" title="Languages of the Caucasus">Caucasian languages</a> in much of the <a href="/wiki/Caucasus" title="Caucasus">Caucasus</a> region. Similarly in <a href="/wiki/Europe" title="Europe">Europe</a> and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Urals" title="Urals">Urals</a> the <a href="/wiki/Uralic_languages" title="Uralic languages">Uralic languages</a> (such as Hungarian, Finnish, Estonian etc.) remain, as does <a href="/wiki/Basque_language" title="Basque language">Basque</a>, a pre-Indo-European isolate.
</p><p>Despite being unaware of their common linguistic origin, diverse groups of Indo-European speakers continued to culturally dominate and often replace the indigenous languages of the western two-thirds of Eurasia. By the beginning of the <a href="/wiki/Common_Era" title="Common Era">Common Era</a>, Indo-European peoples controlled almost the entirety of this area: the Celts western and central Europe, the Romans southern Europe, the Germanic peoples northern Europe, the Slavs eastern Europe, the Iranian peoples most of western and central Asia and parts of eastern Europe, and the Indo-Aryan peoples in the <a href="/wiki/Indian_subcontinent" title="Indian subcontinent">Indian subcontinent</a>, with the <a href="/wiki/Tocharians" title="Tocharians">Tocharians</a> inhabiting the Indo-European frontier in western China. By the medieval period, only the <a href="/wiki/Semitic_languages" title="Semitic languages">Semitic</a>, <a href="/wiki/Dravidian_languages" title="Dravidian languages">Dravidian</a>, <a href="/wiki/Languages_of_the_Caucasus" title="Languages of the Caucasus">Caucasian</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Uralic_languages" title="Uralic languages">Uralic languages</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Language_isolate" title="Language isolate">language isolate</a> <a href="/wiki/Basque_language" title="Basque language">Basque</a> remained of the (relatively) <a href="/wiki/Paleo-European_languages" title="Paleo-European languages">indigenous languages of Europe</a> and the western half of Asia.
</p><p>Despite medieval invasions by <a href="/wiki/Eurasian_nomads" title="Eurasian nomads">Eurasian nomads</a>, a group to which the Proto-Indo-Europeans had once belonged, Indo-European expansion reached another peak in the <a href="/wiki/Early_modern_period" title="Early modern period">early modern period</a> with the dramatic increase in the population of the <a href="/wiki/Indian_subcontinent" title="Indian subcontinent">Indian subcontinent</a> and European expansionism throughout the globe during the <a href="/wiki/Age_of_Discovery" title="Age of Discovery">Age of Discovery</a>, as well as the continued replacement and assimilation of surrounding non-Indo-European languages and peoples due to increased state centralization and <a href="/wiki/Nationalism" title="Nationalism">nationalism</a>. These trends compounded throughout the modern period due to the general global <a href="/wiki/Population_growth" title="Population growth">population growth</a> and the results of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/European_colonization" title="European colonization">European colonization</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Western_Hemisphere" title="Western Hemisphere">Western Hemisphere</a> and <a href="/wiki/Oceania" title="Oceania">Oceania</a>, leading to an explosion in the number of Indo-European speakers as well as the territories inhabited by them.
</p><p>Due to colonization and the modern dominance of Indo-European languages in the fields of politics, global science, technology, education, finance, and sports, even many modern countries whose populations largely speak non-Indo-European languages have Indo-European languages as official languages, and the majority of the global population speaks at least one Indo-European language. The overwhelming majority of <a href="/wiki/Languages_used_on_the_Internet" title="Languages used on the Internet">languages used on the Internet</a> are Indo-European, with <a href="/wiki/English_language" title="English language">English</a> continuing to lead the group; English in general has in many respects <a href="/wiki/English_as_a_lingua_franca" title="English as a lingua franca">become the <i>lingua franca</i></a> of global communication.
</p>
<div style="clear:both;"></div>

<li id="cite_note-Lomonosov-6"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-Lomonosov_6-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a class="external text" href="http://feb-web.ru/feb/lomonos/texts/lo0/lo7/lo7-5952.htm" rel="nofollow">M.V. Lomonosov (drafts for <i>Russian Grammar</i>, published 1755). In: Complete Edition, Moscow, 1952, vol. 7, pp. 652–59</a>:
Представимъ долготу времени, которою сіи языки раздѣлились. ... Польской и россійской языкъ коль давно раздѣлились! Подумай же, когда курляндской! Подумай же, когда латинской, греч., нѣм., росс. О глубокая древность! [Imagine the depth of time when these languages separated! ... Polish and Russian separated so long ago! Now think how long ago [this happened to] Kurlandic! Think when [this happened to] Latin, Greek, German, and Russian! Oh, great antiquity!]</span>
</li>

<li id="cite_note-18"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-18">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation book cs1" id="CITEREFCurtis2011">Curtis, Matthew Cowan (30 November 2011). <a class="external text" href="https://eric.ed.gov/?id=ED546136" rel="nofollow"><i>Slavic–Albanian Language Contact, Convergence, and Coexistence</i></a>. Retrieved <span class="nowrap">31 March</span> 2017</span>. <q>So while linguists may debate about the ties between Albanian and older languages of the Balkans, and while most Albanians may take the genealogical connection to Illyrian as incontrovertible, the fact remains that there is simply insufficient evidence to connect Illyrian, Thracian, or Dacian with any language, including Albanian</q></cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.btitle=Slavic%E2%80%93Albanian+Language+Contact%2C+Convergence%2C+and+Coexistence&amp;rft.pages=18&amp;rft.pub=ProQuest+LLC&amp;rft.date=2011-11-30&amp;rft.isbn=978-1-267-58033-7&amp;rft.aulast=Curtis&amp;rft.aufirst=Matthew+Cowan&amp;rft_id=https%3A%2F%2Feric.ed.gov%2F%3Fid%3DED546136&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AIndo-European+languages"></span></span>
</li>

<li id="cite_note-20"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-20">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation web cs1" id="CITEREFGüterbock">Güterbock, Hans G. <a class="external text" href="http://oi.uchicago.edu/pdf/ar/61-70/65-66/65-66_CHD.pdf" rel="nofollow">"The Hittite Computer Analysis Project"</a> <span class="cs1-format">(PDF)</span>.</cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=unknown&amp;rft.btitle=The+Hittite+Computer+Analysis+Project&amp;rft.aulast=G%C3%BCterbock&amp;rft.aufirst=Hans+G.&amp;rft_id=http%3A%2F%2Foi.uchicago.edu%2Fpdf%2Far%2F61-70%2F65-66%2F65-66_CHD.pdf&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AIndo-European+languages"></span></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-21"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-21">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">such as Schleicher 1861, Szemerényi 1957, Collinge 1985, and Beekes 1995</span>
